Khiyar al-Wasf: Type vs Genus in Cloth Sales
Lists three cloth examples for khiyar al-wasf rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a change in genus (jins) and a change in type (naw') in khiyar al-wasf and state the resulting ruling (void, option, or valid without option).

Core Idea
Change in genus voids the sale; change in type gives the buyer the option to cancel; change in a subordinate part has no effect on validity.

This lesson examines three specific cloth examples from the passage to understand how khiyar al-wasf applies when the stipulated description differs from the actual item. The rulings hinge on whether the difference is in the fundamental genus, a subtype within that genus, or a subordinate component.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stipulated description
Read each example and note what quality was stipulated (e.g., dyed with safflower, six-thread, silk).
2
Compare with the actual item
Determine how the actual item differed (e.g., white instead of safflower-dyed, five-thread instead of six-thread, cotton warp with silk weft).
3
Classify the difference
Is the difference in genus (jins), type (naw'), or a subordinate part? Genus differences void the sale; type differences give the buyer an option; subordinate differences do not affect validity.
Genus refers to the fundamental category (e.g., cloth vs. non-cloth), type refers to a subtype within that genus (e.g., six-thread vs. five-thread), and subordinate refers to a part that is not essential to the main item (e.g., warp in silk cloth).
4
Apply the ruling
Once classified, state the ruling: void (fasid), valid with option (khiyar), or valid without option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى ثوباً على أنه مصبوغ بالعصفر فإذا هو أبيض. جاز البيع ويخير بخلاف عكسه فإنه يفسد ولو اشترى كرباساً على أن سداه ألف فإذا هو ألف ومائة سلم الثوب إلى المشتري لأنه زيادة وصف. ولو اشترى ثوباً على أنه سداسي فإذا هو خماسي خير المشتري إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن وإن شاء ترك لأنه اختلاف نوع لا جنس فلا يفسده ولو باع ثوباً على أنه خز فإذا لحمته خز وسداه قطن جاز البيع لأن السدى تبع للحمة ولو اشترى سويقاً على أن البائع لته بمن من سمن وتقابضا والمشتري ينظر إليه فظهر أنه لته بنصف من جاز البيع ولا خيار للمشتري لأنه هذا مما يعرف بالعيان فإذا عاينه انتفى الغرور ، وهو كما لو اشترى صابوناً على أنه متخذ كذا من جرة من الدهن ثم ظهر أنه من أقل من ذلك والمشتري كان ينظر إلى الصابون وقت الشراء، وكذا لو اشترى قميصاً على أنه اتخذ من عشرة أذرع وهو ينظر إليه فإذا هو من تسعة جاز البيع ولا خيار للمشتري. ولو باع أرضاً على أنها غير خراجية فإذا هي خراجية فسد البيع، وينبغي أن يكون الجواب على التفصيل إن علم المشتري أنها أرض خراج فسد البيع، وإن لم يكن عالماً بذلك جاز البيع ويخير المشتري . اشترى قلنسوة على أن حشوها قطن فلما فتقها المشتري وجدها صوفاً اختلفوا والصحيح جواز البيع والرجوع بالنقصان لأن الحشو تبع وتغير التبع لا يفسد اهـ. ما في الخانية. والهملاج قال في المصباح هملج البرذون هملجة مشى مشية سهلة في سرعة. وقال في مختصر العين: الهملجة حسن سير الدابة وكلهم قالوا في اسم الفاعل هملاج بكسر الهاء للذكر والأنثى بمقتضى أن اسم الفاعل لم يجئ على قياسه وهو مهملج اهـ. اعلم أن اشتراط متخذ وهي . بيع بينهما وهذا قوله «ولو اشترى بقرة على أنها حبلى الخ» حيث ذكر هناك أن البيع فاسد وهنا أنه جائز ولعله على رواية الحسن كما يأتي قريباً تأمل قوله: (ولو اشترى على أنه بغل الخ) إنما جاز بدون الخيار لكونها من جنس واحد والمشار إليه خير من المسمى على وفق ما قرره من الأصل فتأمل.
And if he buys a cloth on condition it is dyed with safflower but it is white, the sale is valid and he has the option, unlike the opposite (white cloth dyed) which voids. And if he buys canvas on condition its warp is a thousand [threads] but it is a thousand and one hundred, the cloth is delivered to the buyer because it is an increase in quality. And if he buys a cloth on condition it is six-thread but it is five-thread, the buyer has the option: if he wishes he takes it for the full price, if he wishes he leaves it, because it is a difference in type not genus, so it does not void. And if he sells a cloth on condition it is silk but its weft is silk and its warp is cotton, the sale is valid because the warp is subordinate to the weft. And if he buys sawiq on condition the seller mixed it with a mann of fat, and they take possession and the buyer is looking at it, then it appears he mixed it with half a mann, the sale is valid and the buyer has no option, because this is something known by sight, so if he saw it, deception is eliminated. It is like if he buys soap on condition it is made from such-and-such a jar of oil, then it appears it is from less than that, and the buyer was looking at the soap at the time of purchase. Likewise, if he buys a shirt on condition it is made from ten cubits and he is looking at it, then it is from nine, the sale is valid and the buyer has no option. And if he sells land on condition it is non-kharaj land, but it is kharaj land, the sale is void. And it should be that the answer is detailed: if the buyer knew it is kharaj land, the sale is void; if he did not know, the sale is valid and the buyer has the option. He bought a cap on condition its stuffing is cotton, then when the buyer opened it he found it wool. They differed, and the correct view is the validity of the sale and recourse for the deficiency, because the stuffing is a subordinate, and a change in the subordinate does not void. End of what is in al-Khaniyya. And al-hamlaj: al-Misbah said the pacer horse paces a hamlaja, a smooth fast gait. And in Mukhtasar al-ʿAyn: al-hamlaja is good gait of the animal, and they all said the active participle is hamlaj with kasra on the ha for masculine and feminine, by virtue that the active participle does not come on its pattern, and it is muhmalaj. End. Know that the stipulation of (something) ... and this is his saying: 'And if he buys a cow on condition she is pregnant' where it was mentioned there that the sale is false and here that it is valid, and perhaps it is according to the narration of al-Hasan as will come soon. Ponder. His saying: 'And if he buys on condition it is a mule etc.' it is valid without option because it is of one genus and the pointed-to object is better than the named according to what he established from the principle. So ponder.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This lengthy passage presents a series of concrete examples from various Hanafi sources (al-Khaniyya, al-Misbah, etc.) to illustrate the application of khiyar al-wasf. The examples cover: cloth dyed with safflower vs white (the opposite gives khiyar or voids depending on direction), canvas with warp count increase (no khiyar because improvement), cloth type (six-thread vs five-thread gives khiyar because type difference within same genus), silk cloth with cotton warp (valid because warp is subordinate). For consumables like sawiq (barley porridge) mixed with fat, if the buyer sees it at purchase and the amount is less, no khiyar because the defect is visible. Similarly for soap and shirts where the buyer sees the item. Land question: if kharaj status changes, the ruling depends on buyer's knowledge; if he knew, sale voids; if not, valid with khiyar. Cap stuffing: stuffing is subordinate, so change gives only claim for deficiency, not voiding. The passage ends with commentary on the earlier statements, noting potential contradictions (pregnant cow) and explaining the principle for the mule example.

Key Terms
الجنس (al-jins): Genus: the broad category of an item (e.g., cloth, not animal).
النوع (al-naw'): Type: a subdivision within a genus (e.g., different thread counts).
التبع (al-taba'): Subordinate part: a non-essential component that is considered incidental to the main item.
خيار الوصف (khiyar al-wasf): Option of description: the buyer's right to cancel a sale if the item does not match the stipulated description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the cloth examples, which type of difference gives the buyer the option (khiyar) to cancel the sale?
A) Difference in genus
B) Difference in type
C) Difference in subordinate part
D) Any difference

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-wasf, a change in genus voids the sale, a change in type gives the buyer an option, and a change in a subordinate part does not affect validity.

Khiyar and Visible Items: No Option When Defect is Apparent
Provides three examples where visual inspection removes khiyar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that when the buyer sees the item at the time of purchase, any subsequent discovery of a lesser quantity or quality does not grant khiyar.

Core Idea
If the buyer sees the item and the deficiency is visible at purchase, there is no khiyar because there is no deception.

The passage gives three examples (sawiq, soap, shirt) where the buyer visually inspects the item at the time of contract. Even if the actual quantity of an ingredient or the cloth size is less than stipulated, the sale is valid and the buyer has no option. The rationale is that the defect was apparent by sight, so there was no deception (ghurur).

Guided Path
1
Confirm the buyer's inspection
Check if the passage explicitly states that the buyer was looking at the item at the time of purchase.
2
Identify the discrepancy
What was stipulated vs what was actually delivered? (e.g., sawiq mixed with 1 mann fat but found 1/2 mann; shirt made from 10 cubits but found 9.)
3
Determine visibility
Could the discrepancy have been noticed by the buyer upon inspection? In these examples, the buyer saw the actual item (sawiq, soap, shirt) and could see the deficiency.
4
Apply the ruling
Since the defect was visible, the sale is valid and no khiyar. The buyer had the opportunity to detect and reject before concluding.

Source Text

He bought a cap on condition its stuffing is cotton, then when the buyer opened it he found it wool. They differed, and the correct view is the validity of the sale and recourse for the deficiency, because the stuffing is a subordinate, and a change in the subordinate does not void. End of what is in al-Khaniyya. And al-hamlaj: al-Misbah said the pacer horse paces a hamlaja, a smooth fast gait. And in Mukhtasar al-ʿAyn: al-hamlaja is good gait of the animal, and they all said the active participle is hamlaj with kasra on the ha for masculine and feminine, by virtue that the active participle does not come on its pattern, and it is muhmalaj. End. Know that the stipulation of (something) ... and this is his saying: 'And if he buys a cow on condition she is pregnant' where it was mentioned there that the sale is false and here that it is valid, and perhaps it is according to the narration of al-Hasan as will come soon. Ponder. His saying: 'And if he buys on condition it is a mule etc.' it is valid without option because it is of one genus and the pointed-to object is better than the named according to what he established from the principle. So ponder.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This lengthy passage presents a series of concrete examples from various Hanafi sources (al-Khaniyya, al-Misbah, etc.) to illustrate the application of khiyar al-wasf. The examples cover: cloth dyed with safflower vs white (the opposite gives khiyar or voids depending on direction), canvas with warp count increase (no khiyar because improvement), cloth type (six-thread vs five-thread gives khiyar because type difference within same genus), silk cloth with cotton warp (valid because warp is subordinate). For consumables like sawiq (barley porridge) mixed with fat, if the buyer sees it at purchase and the amount is less, no khiyar because the defect is visible. Similarly for soap and shirts where the buyer sees the item. Land question: if kharaj status changes, the ruling depends on buyer's knowledge; if he knew, sale voids; if not, valid with khiyar. Cap stuffing: stuffing is subordinate, so change gives only claim for deficiency, not voiding. The passage ends with commentary on the earlier statements, noting potential contradictions (pregnant cow) and explaining the principle for the mule example.

Key Terms
الغرور (al-ghurur): Deception: misleading the buyer by hiding a defect.
العيان (al-ʿiyān): Sight or visual inspection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the buyer have no khiyar when he sees the sawiq and later finds less fat?
A) Because the difference is in a subordinate part.
B) Because the buyer did not stipulate the quantity.
C) Because the deficiency was visible and deception is eliminated.
D) Because the seller has a right to adjust the quantity.

Key Takeaway
When a buyer visually inspects an item at purchase, any subsequent discovery of a visible deficiency does not grant khiyar because there was no deception.

Khiyar and Land: Kharaj Status and Buyer's Knowledge
Provides a detailed ruling on land and kharaj status with a condition on buyer's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling for khiyar al-wasf when land is sold with a stipulation about its kharaj status, distinguishing between the buyer's knowledge and ignorance.

Core Idea
If land sold as non-kharaj is actually kharaj land, the ruling depends on whether the buyer knew it was kharaj land: void if he knew, valid with option if he did not.

This passage addresses a specific case: land sold with a stipulation that it is non-kharaj (non-taxable) but found to be kharaj (taxable land). The ruling is not a simple void or valid; it is detailed based on the buyer's prior knowledge. If the buyer knew the land was kharaj, the sale is void because the stipulation contradicts his knowledge. If he did not know, the sale is valid with a khiyar (option) to cancel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stipulation
The seller stipulates that the land is 'non-kharaj' (i.e., not subject to kharaj tax).
2
Determine the actual status
The land turns out to be kharaj land (taxable).
3
Check the buyer's knowledge
Did the buyer know at the time of contract that the land was kharaj? The answer leads to two different rulings.
4
Apply the appropriate ruling
If buyer knew: sale is void (fasid). If buyer did not know: sale is valid with khiyar (buyer has option to cancel).
The rationale: if the buyer knew, the stipulation is a lie and the contract is based on falsehood; if he did not know, he was deceived and has an option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى ثوباً على أنه مصبوغ بالعصفر فإذا هو أبيض. جاز البيع ويخير بخلاف عكسه فإنه يفسد ولو اشترى كرباساً على أن سداه ألف فإذا هو ألف ومائة سلم الثوب إلى المشتري لأنه زيادة وصف. ولو اشترى ثوباً على أنه سداسي فإذا هو خماسي خير المشتري إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن وإن شاء ترك لأنه اختلاف نوع لا جنس فلا يفسده ولو باع ثوباً على أنه خز فإذا لحمته خز وسداه قطن جاز البيع لأن السدى تبع للحمة ولو اشترى سويقاً على أن البائع لته بمن من سمن وتقابضا والمشتري ينظر إليه فظهر أنه لته بنصف من جاز البيع ولا خيار للمشتري لأنه هذا مما يعرف بالعيان فإذا عاينه انتفى الغرور ، وهو كما لو اشترى صابوناً على أنه متخذ كذا من جرة من الدهن ثم ظهر أنه من أقل من ذلك والمشتري كان ينظر إلى الصابون وقت الشراء، وكذا لو اشترى قميصاً على أنه اتخذ من عشرة أذرع وهو ينظر إليه فإذا هو من تسعة جاز البيع ولا خيار للمشتري. ولو باع أرضاً على أنها غير خراجية فإذا هي خراجية فسد البيع، وينبغي أن يكون الجواب على التفصيل إن علم المشتري أنها أرض خراج فسد البيع، وإن لم يكن عالماً بذلك جاز البيع ويخير المشتري . اشترى قلنسوة على أن حشوها قطن فلما فتقها المشتري وجدها صوفاً اختلفوا والصحيح جواز البيع والرجوع بالنقصان لأن الحشو تبع وتغير التبع لا يفسد اهـ. ما في الخانية. والهملاج قال في المصباح هملج البرذون هملجة مشى مشية سهلة في سرعة. وقال في مختصر العين: الهملجة حسن سير الدابة وكلهم قالوا في اسم الفاعل هملاج بكسر الهاء للذكر والأنثى بمقتضى أن اسم الفاعل لم يجئ على قياسه وهو مهملج اهـ. اعلم أن اشتراط متخذ وهي . بيع بينهما وهذا قوله «ولو اشترى بقرة على أنها حبلى الخ» حيث ذكر هناك أن البيع فاسد وهنا أنه جائز ولعله على رواية الحسن كما يأتي قريباً تأمل قوله: (ولو اشترى على أنه بغل الخ) إنما جاز بدون الخيار لكونها من جنس واحد والمشار إليه خير من المسمى على وفق ما قرره من الأصل فتأمل.
And if he buys a cloth on condition it is dyed with safflower but it is white, the sale is valid and he has the option, unlike the opposite (white cloth dyed) which voids. Subordinate Parts in Sales: No Void for Change in Stuffing
Provides a ruling on cap stuffing as an example of subordinate parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a change in a subordinate part (taba') of an item does not void the sale, but only gives a claim for deficiency.

Core Idea
A change in a subordinate part does not void the sale; the buyer may only claim compensation for the deficiency.

The final example in the passage deals with a cap (qalansuwah) where the stuffing is stipulated to be cotton but is found to be wool. The Hanafi scholars differed, but the correct view according to the source is that the sale is valid and the buyer only claims the price difference. The reason: the stuffing is a subordinate part (taba'), and a change in a subordinate does not invalidate the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stipulation and actual
What was stipulated (cotton stuffing) vs what was found (wool stuffing).
2
Classify the part
Is the stuffing a principal part or subordinate? The passage explicitly says 'al-hashw taba' (stuffing is subordinate).
3
Apply the principle
A change in a subordinate part does not void the contract. The sale is valid but the buyer may claim the difference in value (naqis).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars said the sale is void, but the correct (sahih) view is validity with deficiency claim.

Mistaken Gender in Sale: Tatarkhaniyya Case and Conditions
Critical refinement of istihsan vs qiyas in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a sale when the seller misidentifies the gender of the slave, and distinguish between a condition that voids the contract and a desirable description that does not.

Core Idea
A sale of a person as a slave-girl but it is a boy is valid, with option; a milking condition is a desirable description, not voiding.

This lesson examines a case from al-Tatarkhaniyya where a seller points to a person as a slave-girl but it is a boy. The Hanafi scholars apply qiyas, not istihsan, leading to a valid sale with an option for the buyer. The lesson also explores a related intra-Hanafi dispute over stipulating that an animal is a milking animal, clarifying the distinction between conditions that are part of the essence and those that are mere desirable descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core case and its ruling
The case: selling a person as a slave-girl, but it is a boy. Ruling: sale valid, buyer has the option (khiyar) due to the mistake.
The ruling is based on qiyas; no istihsan is applied here.
2
Understand the method applied
Why no istihsan? The scholars did not adopt istihsan; they stuck to qiyas. The qiyas is that a person can be sold, and the mistake gives an option.
This shows that istihsan is not used automatically; qiyas can prevail.
3
Distinguish the secondary issue
The passage also discusses buying a milking animal on condition. There is an ikhtilaf: Ibn Sama'a (from Muhammad) says the condition voids the sale because milk is considered a 'principle' (asl). Al-Tahawi says it does not void because it is a desirable description (sifa muraghghaba).
Ibn Sama'a's view: milk is part of the essence, so condition on it voids. Al-Tahawi: similar to stipulating a pacer horse.
4
Apply the analogy to other cases
The passage analogizes to stipulating a horse is a pacer or a dog is a hunter – these are valid conditions. This helps define 'desirable description'.
If the condition is about a core attribute, it may void; if about a desirable trait, it is valid with option when not fulfilled.
5
Formulate the mufta bihi
For the mistaken gender case: sale valid with option. For the milking condition: the text leans towards al-Tahawi's view (valid) based on the pacer horse analogy, but does not definitively choose.
Later Hanafi practice tends to follow al-Tahawi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتارخانية : إذا باع من آخر شخصاً على أنها جارية وأشار إليها فإذا هو غلام فلا استحسان أخذ به علماؤنا، والقياس أن ينعقد به البيع ويكون للمشتري الخيار، ثم ذكر الأصل المنقول عن المجتبى وبقية التفاريع قوله : (إلى هنا كلام (المعراج أي من عند قوله في أول المقولة، وفي المعراج إلى هنا من كلامه لكن ذكر المؤلف ما ليس منه وهو قوله والأصل أن القول إلى قوله وفي النوازل، وما ذكره هنا من أنه لو اشتراها على أنها حلوب يفسد ذكر في فتح القدير أنه رواية ابن سماعة عن محمد قال: لأن المشروط هنا أصل من وجه وهو اللبن. ونقل في المعراج قبل هذا عن الطحاوي أنه لا يفسد لأنه وصف مرغوب، وكذا ذكره في الفتح وقال : كما إذا شرط في الفرس أنه هملاج وفي الكلب أنه صائد حيث يصح . قوله : ولو باع جارية على أنها ما ولدت الخ)
And in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If someone sells another person as a slave-girl and points to her, but he is a boy, then there is no istihsan adopted by our scholars. The analogy (qiyas) is that the sale is concluded and the buyer has the option. Then he mentioned the principle transmitted from al-Mujtaba and the rest of the sub-branches. His saying: 'Up to here is the speech of al-Mi'raj', i.e., from his saying at the beginning of the discourse. And in al-Mi'raj, up to here is from his speech, but the author mentioned what is not from it, namely his saying: 'And the principle is that the statement ... up to his saying: and in al-Nawazil.' And what he mentioned here that if he buys her on condition she is a milking animal, it voids, is mentioned in Fath al-Qadir as a narration of Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad, who said: because what is stipulated here is a principle in one respect, namely milk. And it is transmitted in al-Mi'raj before this from al-Tahawi that it does not void because it is a desirable description. And likewise it is mentioned in al-Fath, and he said: just as if he stipulates in a horse that it is a pacer, and in a dog that it is a hunter, where it is valid. His saying: 'And if he sells a slave-girl on condition she has never given birth, etc.'
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the commentary on al-Mi'raj. It begins with a case from al-Tatarkhaniyya: if someone sells a person pointing to her as a slave-girl, but it turns out to be a boy (ghulam), the Hanafi scholars do not apply istihsan (equitable preference) in this case. Instead, qiyas (analogy) applies: the sale is concluded (because a person can be sold) but the buyer has the option (khiyar) due to the mistake in gender. The passage then discusses the authorship boundaries of al-Mi'raj, noting that some parts are not from al-Mi'raj but added by the author of al-Bahr. It also reconciles a difference: the ruling that buying a milking animal (halub) on condition is void is from Ibn Sama'a's narration from Muhammad, because milk is considered a 'principle' (asl) in one sense. But al-Tahawi holds it does not void because it is a desirable description, similar to stipulating that a horse is a pacer or a dog is a hunter. The latter view seems more consistent. The passage ends by referring back to the earlier condition about a slave-girl who has never given birth.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Equitable preference, a Hanafi juristic method that departs from strict qiyas for a stronger reason.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical deduction; applying the ruling of an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause (illa).
الخيار (al-khiyar): Option; the right of the buyer to either confirm or cancel the sale upon discovering a defect or misdescription.
وصف مرغوب (sifa muraghghaba): Desirable description; an attribute that is sought after but not part of the essence of the item.
أصل من وجه (asl min wajh): A principle in one respect; something that is considered a core attribute from a certain perspective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Tatarkhaniyya case, if someone sells a person as a slave-girl but it is a boy, what is the ruling based on qiyas?
A) The sale is void.
B) The sale is valid and the buyer has no option.
C) The sale is valid and the buyer has the option.
D) The sale is void because of mistake in gender.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a sale with a mistaken description of an essential attribute is valid but gives the buyer an option; conditions on desirable descriptions are valid and do not void the contract.

Disclaimer of Defect Does Not Invalidate the Sale
States a ruling and gives an example of disclaimer of defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a general disclaimer of defects (tabarrī) in a sale and apply it to determine whether the buyer retains the option of defect.

Core Idea
A seller's disclaimer of defects is valid and does not void the sale; the buyer's option activates only if a defect appears.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq addresses a common concern: does a seller's blanket statement 'I disclaim all defects' ruin the sale? The clear ruling is that the sale remains valid. The example shows that if the buyer finds the item free of defects, he has no right to return it. Let's unpack why this ruling is important for contracts and how it balances seller protection with buyer rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the seller's tabarrī (disclaimer of defects). The ruling is that it does not invalidate (lā yufsid) the sale — it is valid (sahih).
Focus on the verb 'yufsid' — the disclaimer does not cause fasād.
2
Understand the conditional trigger for the buyer's option
The buyer's khiyār al-‛ayb (option for defect) is conditioned on finding an actual defect. If no defect is found ('idhā lam yajidhā mughniyya'), no option exists.
The word 'mughniyya' here means a defect that affects value (e.g., a slave girl who cannot sing).
3
Apply the rationale
The buyer found her free of defect ('salimatan min al-‛ayb'), so the condition for exercising the option is absent. The disclaimer itself does not create an option.
This prevents buyers from using a general disclaimer as a pretext to cancel a perfectly good sale.
4
Distinguish from specific disclaimers
If the seller excludes a particular defect from the disclaimer, the buyer retains the option for that defect. The general rule applies only to blanket disclaimers.
This nuance is not in the passage but follows from Hanafi principles.
5
Contrast with non-Hanafi views
Some schools (e.g., Shafi‘i) hold that a blanket disclaimer is invalid because it involves uncertainty (gharar). Hanafis uphold it because the disclaimer makes the sale transparent and the buyer assumes the risk.
The passage implicitly supports the Hanafi position; we will explore the ikhtilāf in the analysis block.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التبري من العيب لا يفسد، فإذا لم يجدها مغنية لا خيار له لأنه وجدها سالمة من العيب.
Disclaimer of a defect does not invalidate [the sale]. So if he did not find her defective, there is no option for him because he found her free of defect.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart) or defects. It states that when a seller sells an item disclaiming liability for defects (tabarrī min al-‛ayb), the sale remains valid; it is not spoiled by the disclaimer itself. The example: if the buyer later finds the item free of defect, he has no option to return it because the condition for exercising the option—the presence of a defect—is not met. The disclaimer only protects the seller if a defect appears; if none appears, the buyer cannot claim ignorance or deception.

Key Terms
التبري (al-tabarrī): The seller's act of disclaiming liability for defects in the sold item.
الخيار (al-khiyār): The option to cancel or confirm a sale upon discovery of a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when a seller makes a general disclaimer of defects and the buyer later finds the item free of any defect?
A) The sale is invalid because the disclaimer is impermissible.
B) The buyer has the option to return the item due to the disclaimer.
C) The sale is valid and the buyer has no option to return.
D) The buyer must return the item because the disclaimer implies a defect.

Key Takeaway
A general disclaimer of defects is valid and does not invalidate the sale; the buyer's option (khiyār al-‛ayb) is contingent on the presence of an actual defect.

Misdescription in Dye: White vs. Dyed Cloth
Ruling on misdescription of dye

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a cloth sold with a dye description is found otherwise, and distinguish between cases where the sale is valid with option vs. void.

Core Idea
Selling a dyed cloth as white voids the sale; selling a white cloth as dyed gives a valid sale with option.

In commercial transactions, description (wasf) matters. Hanafis differentiate based on whether the misdescription adds or removes value. This lesson examines the classic case of cloth sold as dyed but found white, and vice versa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First scenario: buyer stipulates dyed (white found). Second scenario: buyer stipulates white (dyed found).
The text explicitly contrasts these.
2
Apply the rule of qualitative increase vs. decrease
When the found item has less than described (dye missing), the sale is valid with option. When it has more (dye present on white), the sale is void because it imposes an unwanted enhancement.
The key is whether the discrepancy is a ziyadat wasf (increase in attribute) or nuqsan (decrease).
3
Derive the legal consequence
Valid sale with khiyar (option) for the buyer in the first case; fasid (void) sale in the second.
The buyer's option allows acceptance or rejection at full price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى ثوباً على أنه مصبوغ بالعصفر فإذا هو أبيض. جاز البيع ويخير بخلاف عكسه فإنه يفسد. ولو اشترى كرباساً على أن سداه ألف فإذا هو ألف ومائة سلم الثوب إلى المشتري لأنه زيادة وصف. ولو اشترى ثوباً على أنه سداسي فإذا هو خماسي خير المشتري إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن وإن شاء ترك لأنه اختلاف نوع لا جنس فلا يفسده. ولو باع ثوباً على أنه خز فإذا لحمته خز وسداه قطن جاز البيع لأن السدى تبع للحمة.
And if he bought a garment on the condition that it was dyed with safflower, then it turned out white, the sale is valid and he has an option, contrary to its opposite, for then it is void. And if he bought coarse cloth on the condition that its warp is a thousand [threads], then it turned out to be a thousand and one hundred, the cloth is delivered to the buyer because it is an increase in quality. And if he bought a garment on the condition that it is six-span, then it turned out to be five-span, the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes it with the full price, and if he wishes, he leaves it, because it is a difference in type not genus, so it does not void it. And if he sold a garment on the condition that it is khazz [silk-wool blend], then its weft is khazz and its warp is cotton, the sale is valid because the warp is subordinate to the weft.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage contains four related rulings on sale with description (bi-shart al-wasf). First: If a garment is sold as dyed with safflower (a red dye) but is found white, the sale is valid but the buyer has the option to return it (khiyar al-ru'ya or khiyar al-‛ayb). The opposite case—sold as white but found dyed—renders the sale void (fasid) because the dye adds value, constituting an enhancement the buyer did not pay for. Second: If coarse cloth (karbas) is sold with a specific warp count (1000 threads) but is found to have 1100, the item goes to the buyer without option because the increase is a qualitative improvement (ziyadat wasf), not a different genus. Third: If a garment is sold as six-span (a measure) but found to be five-span, the buyer has the option: he may take it for the full price or reject it. This is because the difference is in type (naw‛) but not in genus (jins), so the sale is not void but the buyer is given a choice. Fourth: If a garment is sold as khazz (a mixed silk-wool fabric) but its warp is cotton while the weft is khazz, the sale is valid because the warp is subordinate (tabi‛) to the weft, and the primary material (the weft) determines the description. These rulings illustrate the principle that misdescription affects the sale differently depending on whether it is a qualitative increase, a change in type, or a subordinate component.

Key Terms
وصف (wasf): A quality or attribute of the sold item that is stipulated in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a cloth is sold as being dyed with safflower but is found to be white, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is void (fasid) because of the misdescription.
B) The sale is valid and the buyer has no option.
C) The sale is valid but the buyer has an option to return it.
D) The buyer must accept it with a reduction in price.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, the ruling on misdescription depends on whether the difference is a qualitative increase or decrease relative to the description.

Misdescription in Warp Count, Size, and Fabric Composition
Rulings on qualitative misdescription in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rulings for misdescriptions involving subordinate attributes (warp, size, composition) and apply the principle that subordinate parts do not void the sale.

Core Idea
An increase in warp count does not void; a decrease in size gives option; subordinate parts follow the primary in description.

Not all misdescriptions are equal. Hanafi law classifies them by whether the attribute is part of the essence or subordinate, and whether the change is an increase or decrease. This lesson covers three cases from the text.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the warp count case
If sold as a specific warp count but found with more threads, the item goes to the buyer because it is a qualitative increase.
The increase is an improvement (ziyādat wasf).
2
Analyze the size case
If sold as six-span but found five-span, the buyer has an option because the size is a fundamental attribute and the decrease is not trivial.
The difference is in type (naw‛), not genus (jins).
3
Analyze the fabric composition case
If sold as khazz but the warp is cotton while weft is khazz, the sale is valid because the warp is subordinate (tābi‛) to the weft.
The primary component determines the description.

Source Text

And if he bought coarse cloth on the condition that its warp is a thousand [threads], then it turned out to be a thousand and one hundred, the cloth is delivered to the buyer because it is an increase in quality. This passage contains four related rulings on sale with description (bi-shart al-wasf). First: If a garment is sold as dyed with safflower (a red dye) but is found white, the sale is valid but the buyer has the option to return it (khiyar al-ru'ya or khiyar al-‛ayb). These rulings illustrate the principle that misdescription affects the sale differently depending on whether it is a qualitative increase, a change in type, or a subordinate component.

Key Terms
سداء (sadā): The warp threads in weaving.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a cloth is sold as khazz (silk-wool blend) and the weft is khazz but the warp is cotton, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is void because the warp is different.
B) The sale is valid because the warp is subordinate to the weft.
C) The sale is valid with an option.
D) The sale is suspended until inspection.

Key Takeaway
When a misdescription involves a subordinate attribute (tābi‛) or a qualitative increase, the sale remains valid; only a decrease in an essential attribute gives the buyer an option.

Sale of Items Known by Sight: When Misdescription Does Not Give an Option
Expands the rule that ‘iyān negates option for misdescription

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that visual inspection (‛iyān) negates the option for misdescription (ghurūr) in sales of seen items, and distinguish the exceptional case of land tax misdescription.

Core Idea
Seeing the item at contract cancels the option for quantity or ingredient misdescription.

In Hanafi sales law, a buyer receives an option (khiyar) if the seller's description turns out false, but only if the buyer was deceived. When the buyer sees the subject matter at the time of contract, visual knowledge removes the possibility of deception regarding visible qualities. This lesson unpacks three clear examples from the text—sawiq, soap, and a shirt—where the buyer’s look nullifies the option, and contrasts them with the special case of land misdescription about tax status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling for seen items
Read the first example: the buyer sees the sawiq, so the sale is valid and no option. State the general principle: visual inspection (‛iyān) removes the option for misdescription in quantities or ingredients.
The ruling applies when the misdescription is about a subordinate aspect that could be seen.
2
Trace the legal reasoning: ‛iyān negates ghurūr
The text says 'because this is something known by sight; when he saw it, deception is negated.' Understand that the option (khiyar) arises from deception (ghurūr). If the buyer sees the item, he cannot claim he was deceived about visible attributes.
Ghurūr is the basis; seeing eliminates it.
3
Apply to the three examples
Work through sawiq (ghee quantity), soap (oil quantity), and shirt (cloth length). In each case, the buyer sees the item, so the sale is valid and no option. Note that the misdescription is about a quantity or ingredient that could be estimated by sight.
The examples are analogous; the principle is consistent.
4
Analyze the land exception
Land described as non-kharaji but actually kharaji: the ruling depends on the buyer's knowledge. If the buyer knew it was kharaji, the sale is void (fasid) because he accepted a false description about a matter that affects religious permissibility. If he did not know, the sale is valid but he has an option.
This case is different because the misdescription concerns the legal status of the land, not a visible quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى سويقاً على أن البائع لته بمن من سمن وتقابضا والمشتري ينظر إليه فظهر أنه لته بنصف من جاز البيع ولا خيار للمشتري لأنه هذا مما يعرف بالعيان فإذا عاينه انتفى الغرور ، وهو كما لو اشترى صابوناً على أنه متخذ كذا من جرة من الدهن ثم ظهر أنه من أقل من ذلك والمشتري كان ينظر إلى الصابون وقت الشراء، وكذا لو اشترى قميصاً على أنه اتخذ من عشرة أذرع وهو ينظر إليه فإذا هو من تسعة جاز البيع ولا خيار للمشتري. ولو باع أرضاً على أنها غير خراجية فإذا هي خراجية فسد البيع، وينبغي أن يكون الجواب على التفصيل إن علم المشتري أنها أرض خراج فسد البيع، وإن لم يكن عالماً بذلك جاز البيع ويخير المشتري.
And if he bought sawiq [barley flour] on the condition that the seller mixed it with a mann of ghee, and they took possession while the buyer was looking at it, then it appeared that he mixed it with half a mann, the sale is valid and there is no option for the buyer because this is something known by sight; when he saw it, deception is negated. It is like if he bought soap on the condition that it was made from such-and-such a jar of oil, then it appeared that it was from less than that, and the buyer was looking at the soap at the time of purchase; similarly, if he bought a shirt on the condition that it was made from ten cubits while he was looking at it, then it turned out to be from nine, the sale is valid and there is no option for the buyer. And if he sold land on the condition that it is non-kharaji [non-taxable], then it turned out to be kharaji, the sale is void. It should be answered in detail: if the buyer knew that it was kharaji land, the sale is void; if he did not know that, the sale is valid and the buyer has an option.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage provides three examples where the buyer sees the item at the time of contract, so a misdescription in a quantity or ingredient does not give an option because the buyer had visual knowledge and was not deceived (ghurur). First: sawiq (roasted barley flour) mixed with ghee: the seller states he mixed it with one mann, but it is actually half a mann. Since the buyer saw the sawiq, he is considered to have accepted it as is, so no option. Second: soap made from a specific amount of oil; again, seeing the soap negates deception. Third: a shirt made from a specific cloth length; seeing it at purchase eliminates the option. However, if the land is described as non-kharaji (meaning no land tax) but is actually kharaji (subject to tax), the ruling depends on the buyer's knowledge. If the buyer knew it was kharaji land and still bought it, the sale is void (fasid) because the description is false and the buyer had a different intention. If the buyer did not know, the sale is valid but he has an option to return it. This distinction is based on the principle that the nature of the land (kharaji or not) affects the religio-legal permissibility of the sale for the buyer (e.g., if he is a Muslim, kharaji land may involve dealing with unjust taxes).

Key Terms
عيان (‛iyān): Visual inspection; something known by sight.
غرور (ghurūr): Deception or fraud that entitles the buyer to an option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases a shirt while seeing it, but the seller says it is made from 10 cubits of cloth, and it turns out to be 9 cubits, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is void because of the misdescription.
B) The sale is valid and the buyer has an option to return it.
C) The sale is valid and there is no option for the buyer.
D) The buyer must pay for the actual cloth length used.

Key Takeaway
Visual inspection at the time of contract removes the option for misdescription in visible attributes, but not for hidden legal characteristics like land tax status.

Cap with Cotton Stuffing Found to be Wool: Validity with Compensation
Ruling on misdescription of a subordinate part in a sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a subordinate attribute (taba‛) is misdescribed, and identify that the sale remains valid with compensation.

Core Idea
A change in a subordinate part does not void the sale; the buyer gets compensation for the deficiency.

The first ruling in the passage addresses a common commercial scenario: when a specific attribute of an item is stipulated but not met. Here, the stuffing of a cap is a subordinate part. Understanding this distinction is key to Hanafi contract law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Stipulation
Determine what was sold (a cap) and what condition was stipulated (cotton stuffing). Recognize that the condition is about a component (stuffing) that is not the main substance of the cap.
The stuffing is a subordinate part (taba‛) of the cap.
2
Recognize the Disclosure
When the buyer opens the cap, he finds wool instead of cotton. This is a breach of the stipulation.
3
Apply the Hanafi Principle on Taba‛
The Hanafi rule is that a change in a subordinate attribute (taba‛) does not invalidate the contract. The sale remains valid (sahih).
This is because the essence (asl) of the item is unchanged.
4
Identify the Remedy
Since the buyer received something less than what was agreed, he is entitled to compensation (naqsān) for the difference in value between a cap with cotton stuffing and one with wool stuffing.
The buyer does not have the option to rescind the sale; only monetary compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشترى قلنسوة على أن حشوها قطن فلما فتقها المشتري وجدها صوفاً اختلفوا والصحيح جواز البيع والرجوع بالنقصان لأن الحشو تبع وتغير التبع لا يفسد. ولو اشترى بقرة على أنها حبلى فظهر أنها غير حامل فسد البيع. ولو اشترى على أنه بغل فإذا هو حمار أو فرس جاز بغير خيار . وإذا باع من آخر شخصاً على أنها جارية وأشار إليها فإذا هو غلام فلا استحسان أخذ به علماؤنا، والقياس أن ينعقد به البيع ويكون للمشتري الخيار.
He bought a cap on the condition that its stuffing is cotton. When the buyer opened it, he found it to be wool. They differed, but the correct [view] is that the sale is valid and he returns for the deficiency because the stuffing is subordinate, and a change in the subordinate does not invalidate. And if he bought a cow on the condition that it is pregnant, then it appears that it is not pregnant, the sale is void. And if he bought [an animal] on the condition that it is a mule, then it turns out to be a donkey or a horse, it is valid without option. And if someone sells another person as a slave girl, pointing to him, then he turns out to be a boy, there is no istihsan that our scholars adopted; the analogy is that the sale is concluded and the buyer has an option.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings on misdescription. First: a cap (qalansuwah) sold with the condition that its stuffing is cotton, but found to be wool. The correct Hanafi view (as per the text) is that the sale is valid, and the buyer may claim compensation for the difference in value (naqsān) because the stuffing is a subordinate part (tab‛); changing a subordinate does not make the sale fasid. This resolves an ikhtilaf among scholars. Second: a cow sold as pregnant but found not pregnant: the sale is void (fasid) because pregnancy is considered an essential attribute (it affects value and purpose). Third: if an animal is sold as a mule but turns out to be a donkey or horse: the sale is valid with no option because all are of the same genus (equine) and the distinction is not considered a change in essence. Fourth: if a person is sold as a female slave (jāriya) but turns out to be a male (ghulām): the text says there is no istihsan (equitable preference) adopted by our scholars; the qiyas (analogy) is that the sale is valid but the buyer has the option (khiyar). This indicates that the default ruling is that misdescription in gender gives an option, but some scholars may differ. The passage highlights the Hanafi approach of distinguishing between essential and subordinate attributes.

Key Terms
تبع (taba‛): A subordinate part or attribute that is not essential to the main item.
نقصان (naqsān): Deficiency; the compensation paid for the difference in value due to the misdescription.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the correct Hanafi ruling when a cap sold with the condition of cotton stuffing is found to be wool?
A) The sale is void.
B) The sale is valid and the buyer has no recourse.
C) The sale is valid and the buyer may claim compensation for the deficiency.
D) The buyer has the option to return the cap.

Key Takeaway
A change in a subordinate attribute (taba‛) does not invalidate the sale; the remedy is compensation for the deficiency in value.

Cow Sold as Pregnant but Found Not Pregnant: Void Sale
Ruling on misdescription of an essential attribute (pregnancy) in a sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that when an essential attribute like pregnancy is misdescribed, the sale is void (fasid) according to Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Pregnancy is an essential attribute; its absence voids the sale.

The second ruling in the passage contrasts with the first. While a subordinate misdescription only triggers compensation, an essential misdescription renders the contract void. Pregnancy is considered an essential attribute because it significantly affects the value and purpose of the animal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Stipulation
The buyer stipulates that the cow is pregnant. This is a condition that goes to the essence of the sale; a pregnant cow is more valuable and may be bought for breeding.
Pregnancy is not a subordinate part; it is a state of the animal.
2
Compare to the Cap Case
Unlike the stuffing of a cap, pregnancy is an essential attribute (asl). The Hanafi principle distinguishes between subordinate and essential attributes.
If an essential condition is unmet, the contract is invalid.
3
Apply the Ruling
Since the attribute is essential and the condition is unmet, the sale is fasid (void). The buyer can rescind the contract.
No compensation is relevant; the contract is null.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشترى قلنسوة على أن حشوها قطن فلما فتقها المشتري وجدها صوفاً اختلفوا والصحيح جواز البيع والرجوع بالنقصان لأن الحشو تبع وتغير التبع لا يفسد. ولو اشترى بقرة على أنها حبلى فظهر أنها غير حامل فسد البيع. ولو اشترى على أنه بغل فإذا هو حمار أو فرس جاز بغير خيار . وإذا باع من آخر شخصاً على أنها جارية وأشار إليها فإذا هو غلام فلا استحسان أخذ به علماؤنا، والقياس أن ينعقد به البيع ويكون للمشتري الخيار.
He bought a cap on the condition that its stuffing is cotton. When the buyer opened it, he found it to be wool. They differed, but the correct [view] is that the sale is valid and he returns for the deficiency because the stuffing is subordinate, and a change in the subordinate does not invalidate. And if he bought a cow on the condition that it is pregnant, then it appears that it is not pregnant, the sale is void. And if he bought [an animal] on the condition that it is a mule, then it turns out to be a donkey or a horse, it is valid without option. And if someone sells another person as a slave girl, pointing to him, then he turns out to be a boy, there is no istihsan that our scholars adopted; the analogy is that the sale is concluded and the buyer has an option.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings on misdescription. First: a cap (qalansuwah) sold with the condition that its stuffing is cotton, but found to be wool. The correct Hanafi view (as per the text) is that the sale is valid, and the buyer may claim compensation for the difference in value (naqsān) because the stuffing is a subordinate part (tab‛); changing a subordinate does not make the sale fasid. This resolves an ikhtilaf among scholars. Second: a cow sold as pregnant but found not pregnant: the sale is void (fasid) because pregnancy is considered an essential attribute (it affects value and purpose). Third: if an animal is sold as a mule but turns out to be a donkey or horse: the sale is valid with no option because all are of the same genus (equine) and the distinction is not considered a change in essence. Fourth: if a person is sold as a female slave (jāriya) but turns out to be a male (ghulām): the text says there is no istihsan (equitable preference) adopted by our scholars; the qiyas (analogy) is that the sale is valid but the buyer has the option (khiyar). This indicates that the default ruling is that misdescription in gender gives an option, but some scholars may differ. The passage highlights the Hanafi approach of distinguishing between essential and subordinate attributes.

Key Terms
فسد (fasada): To be void; invalid.
حبلى (ḥublā): Pregnant.

Key Takeaway
When an essential attribute like pregnancy is misdescribed, the sale is void (fasid).

Mule Sold as Mule but Turns Out to be Donkey or Horse: Valid Without Option
Ruling on misdescription within the same genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that when an animal is sold with a condition of being a mule but it is a donkey or horse, the sale is valid without any option because they are of the same genus.

Core Idea
If the misdescribed item is of the same genus, the sale is valid with no option.

The third ruling presents a scenario where the species is misdescribed but within the same genus. A mule, donkey, and horse are all equines. The Hanafi position is that the sale is valid without any option for the buyer, as the difference does not constitute a fundamental change.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Stipulation and Actual Item
The buyer stipulates that the animal is a mule, but it turns out to be a donkey or a horse. Note that these are different species but belong to the same genus (equine).
2

This is a misdescription of the species, but since the genus is the same (all are riding/working animals), the Hanafis consider the transaction valid without any option.
Contrast with the cow pregnancy case where the attribute was essential.
3
Apply the Ruling
The sale is valid (jāz) and the buyer has no option (lā khiyār) because the difference does not affect the fundamental purpose of the animal.

Person Sold as Female Slave Turns Out to be Male: Valid with Option by Qiyas
Ruling on gender misdescription in a sale of a human being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that when a person is sold as a female slave but is male, the sale is valid but the buyer has an option (khiyar) according to Hanafi qiyas.

Core Idea
Gender is an essential attribute; misdescription gives the buyer an option, not automatic voidance.

The final ruling in the passage deals with a fundamental misdescription: gender. A person is sold as a slave girl (jāriya) but is actually a male (ghulām). The text states that the Hanafi scholars did not adopt istihsān here; instead, they applied qiyas. The analogy (qiyas) yields that the sale is valid (yanʿaqid) but the buyer has an option (khiyār).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Misdescription
The seller points to a person and says 'this is a slave girl', but the person is male. Gender is a fundamental attribute.
This is similar to the cow pregnancy case in being essential, but the ruling differs.
2
Understand the Methodological Choice
The text says 'lā istihsān akhadha bihi ʿulamāʾunā' – our scholars did not adopt istihsān. They used qiyas (analogy) instead.
Istihsān would have voided the sale; qiyas gives an option.
3
Apply the Qiyas Ruling
By qiyas, the sale is valid (yanʿaqid bihi al-bayʿ) but the buyer has the option (khiyār) to confirm or rescind.
This is different from the cow case (void) and the mule case (no option).

Khiyar al-Ru'ya: The Option of Inspection for Unseen Purchases
Chapter opening defining Khiyar al-Ru'ya and its general principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purchasing an unseen item, identify the scope of the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya), and apply the conditions that preserve or nullify it.

Core Idea
Buying unseen is valid, but the buyer automatically has the right to return upon seeing—no stipulation needed.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq opens the chapter on the Option of Inspection (Khiyar al-Ru'ya). It lays down the foundational ruling: a buyer who purchases an item without seeing it may return it after viewing, even if he had previously expressed satisfaction. The text clarifies that this option applies only to tangible assets (a'yan), not to debts or fungibles, and that it is established by law (hukman), not by contractual stipulation. Understanding these principles is essential for correctly applying this khiyar in sales transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and key terms
Locate the statement that buying unseen is permissible (ja'iz) and that the buyer has the right to return. Note the term 'khiyar al-ru'ya' and its definition.
2
Distinguish the scope: a'yan vs. debts
The text specifies that the option applies to tangible assets (a'yan) but not to debts (dyun) like the price or subject matter in salam. Identify the exception for the capital of salam when it is a tangible asset.
Know why debts are excluded: they are not subject to inspection by sight.
3
Examine contracts where the option does NOT apply
List the contracts mentioned (dower, khul', sulh for qisas) and understand why they are not rescindable by return.
These contracts are not pure sales; their cancellation would undo the primary obligation.
4
Analyze the effect of exercising the option and the requirement of seller's knowledge
The return is a rescission (faskh) and does not need court order or seller's consent. However, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the seller must be informed; Muhammad disagrees. Determine the mufta bihi.
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and how the majority's view is relied upon.
5
Identify what nullifies the option
The option is lost if the buyer disposes of the item (e.g., sells it), if it perishes in his hands, or if it becomes impossible to return. Also, the option does not prevent ownership from passing.
Any act inconsistent with the right to return terminates the khiyar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب خيار الرؤية. شراء ما لم يره جائز وله أن يرده إذا رآه وإن رضي قبله ولا خيار لمن باع ما لم يره. الوصف المرغوب فيه إما أن يكون صريحاً أو دلالة. ثم اعلم أن هذا الخيار يثبت للمشتري في شراء الأعيان ولا يثبت في الديون كالمسلم فيه والأثمان. وأما في رأس مال السلم إن كان عيناً فإنه يثبت للبائع أي المسلم إليه الخيار فيه. ولا يثبت في كل عقد لا ينفسخ بالرد كالمهر وبدل الخلع وبدل الصلح عن القصاص. والرد بخيار الرؤية فسخ قبل القبض وبعده، ولا يحتاج إلى قضاء ولا رضا البائع وينفسخ بقوله رددت إلا أنه لا يصلح الرد إلا بعلم البائع عندهما خلافاً للثاني. وهو يثبت حكماً لا بالشرط. ولا يتوقت ولا يمنع وقوع الملك للمشتري حتى إنه لو تصرف فيه جاز تصرفه وبطل خياره ولزمه الثمن، وكذا لو هلك في يده أو صار إلى حال لا يملك فسخه بطل خياره.
Chapter on Option of Inspection. Purchase of what he has not seen is permissible, and he has the right to return it when he sees it, even if he was satisfied before. And there is no option for one who sells what he has not seen. The desirable description is either explicit or by implication. Then know that this option is established for the buyer in the purchase of tangible assets ('ayn) and is not established in debts like the object of salam and prices. As for the capital of salam if it is a tangible asset, then the option is established for the seller, i.e., the Muslim ilayh. And it is not established in any contract that is not dissolved by return, like the dower and the compensation for khul' and the compensation for sulh concerning retaliation. Repudiation by the option of inspection is a rescission before and after possession, and it does not require a court order nor the consent of the seller, and it is dissolved by his saying 'I returned', except that the return is not valid except with the seller's knowledge according to the two [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf], contrary to the second [Muhammad]. And it is established by law not by stipulation. And it is not limited by time and does not prevent the ownership from vesting in the buyer, such that if he disposed of it, his disposition is valid and his option is void and the price becomes binding; similarly, if it perishes in his hand or becomes such that he cannot rescind, his option becomes void.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the chapter on Option of Inspection (Khiyar al-Ru'ya) from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It defines the khiyar and its general rules. The general ruling: buying an unseen item (that is not fungible) is permissible (ja'iz). The buyer has the right to return the item upon seeing it, even if he expressed satisfaction before seeing. The seller, however, has no such option if he sells something he has not seen, because the khiyar is for the buyer only. The 'desirable description' (al-wasf al-marghub fihi) that gives the khiyar can be explicitly stated or implied by context. The option is established only for tangible assets (a'yān), not for debts or currencies (like the price in a sale, or the subject matter in salam). However, in salam, if the capital (ra's al-mal) is a tangible asset, the seller (muslam ilayh) gets the khiyar. The option does not apply to contracts that are not revocable by return, such as dower (mahr), compensation for khul', or sulh for qisas. Repudiation under this khiyar is a rescission (faskh), not a termination, and it takes effect before and after possession. It does not require a court judgment or the seller's consent; the buyer's words 'radadtu' effect the rescission, but according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the return is not valid unless the seller is informed; Muhammad holds otherwise. This khiyar is established by law (hukman) not by stipulation, meaning it exists automatically for the buyer of an unseen item. It is not limited by time, and it does not prevent ownership from passing to the buyer. If the buyer disposes of the item (e.g., sells it), his disposition is valid but the option is lost and the price becomes binding. Similarly, if the item perishes in his hands or becomes such that return is impossible, the option is lost.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ru'ya): The option of inspection; the buyer's right to return an unseen item upon seeing it.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of the contract; it applies here before and after possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following statements regarding khiyar al-ru'ya is correct?
A) It applies to the purchase of debts and currencies as well as tangible assets.
B) It is established by law, not by stipulation, and the buyer may return the item without the seller's consent, but according to the majority he must inform the seller.
C) The buyer loses the option only if he explicitly states that he is satisfied after seeing the item.
D) The seller also has the option if he sold something he had not seen.

Key Takeaway
The option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) is a legally established right for the buyer of an unseen tangible asset, allowing return upon viewing, subject to limitations such as requiring seller's knowledge and being voided by disposal.
