When Sight is Not Sight: Khiyar al-Ru'ya in a Changing World
Details rulings on khiyar al-ru'ya for cloth, houses, and various other sold items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya for cloth and houses evolved due to changing customs, and identify the mufta bihi position for each.

Core Idea
Custom changes the conditions of khiyar al-ru'ya: today, interior must be seen for cloth and houses.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq shows how Hanafi fiqh adapts to changing circumstances. For cloth and houses, the early Hanafi position sufficed with seeing the outside or folded state. However, as custom changed, the mufta bihi shifted to requiring interior sight, following Imam Zufar. The passage also applies the same principle to various other items, illustrating that the goal of khiyar al-ru'ya is to give the buyer actual knowledge (ilm) of the object.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original positions for cloth and house
Note that the author (al-Musannif) states that seeing the folded cloth or the exterior of the house suffices according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). Understand the reasoning: the outer covering reveals the inner, and opening would harm the seller.
This was based on the custom of their time.
2
Recognize the custom change and adoption of Zufar's view
The commentator explains that with changing customs (difference between inside and outside of cloth, variation in house interiors), the view of Imam Zufar has become the mufta bihi: the buyer must see the interior. Confirm that Jami' al-Fusulayn gives fatwa on this.
Zufar's view is relied upon today for both cloth and houses.
3
Understand the underlying principle: actual knowledge (ilm)
The ruling turns on whether the sight gives the buyer certain knowledge of the object. If custom makes the exterior insufficient, then interior sight is required. This is consistent with the purpose of khiyar al-ru'ya.
Other items in the passage also follow this: e.g., oil must be poured out, not seen through a bottle.
4
Apply the principle to other sold items
Review the conditions for gardens, vineyards, pomegranates, oil in bottle, mirror reflection, fish in water, taste and smell items, and musk pod. Each condition is set to ensure the buyer gains knowledge.
Note the exception for items where extraction (musk pod) changes the item, losing the option.
5
Note the cross-madhhab comparison and fatwa hierarchy
The Shafi'i school requires seeing even more details (upper floor, kitchen, etc.) for a house. The Hanafi mufta bihi today is Zufar's view, which is closer to the Shafi'i requirement, but still distinct. The fatwa authority shifts from zahir al-riwaya to later fatwa works.
This demonstrates that the Hanafi school is not static but evolves with custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثوب فاكتفى المصنف ظاهره مطوياً لأن البادئ يعرف ما في الطي فلو شرط فتحه لتضرر البائع بتكسره ونقصان قيمته وبذلك ينقص ثمنه عليه إلا أن يكون له وجهان فلا بد من رؤية كليهما أو يكون في طيه ما يقصد البيوت والأصح أن جواب الكتاب على وفاق عادتهم في الأبنية فإن دورهم لم تكن متفاوتة يومئذ، فأما اليوم فلا بد من الدخول داخل الدار للتفاوت، فالنظر إلى ظاهر لا يوقع العلم بالداخل، وفي جامع الفصولين وبه يفتى. فالحاصل أن المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى اختار قول زفر في الدار وكان ينبغي له اختياره في الثوب فإن المختار قوله فيهما. وشرط بعضهم رؤية العلو والمطبخ والمزبلة وهو الأظهر والأشبه كما قال الشافعي وهو المعتبر في ديار مصر والشام . ولم يذكر المصنف بقية أنواع المبيعات ولا بد من ذكرها قالوا : لا بد في البستان من رؤية ظاهره وباطنه، وفي الكرم لا بد من رؤية عنب الكرم من كل نوع شيئاً، وفي الرمان لا بد من رؤية الحلو والحامض، ولو اشترى دهناً في زجاجة فرؤيته من خارج الزجاجة لا تكفي حتى يصبه في كفه عند أبي حنيفة لأنه لم ير الدهن حقيقة لوجود الحائل. وفي التحفة لو نظر في المرآة فرأى المبيع قالوا : لا يسقط خياره لأنه ما رأى عينه بل رأى مثاله. ولو اشترى سمكاً في ماء يمكن أخذه من غير اصطياد فرآه في قال بعضهم : يسقط خياره لأنه رأى عين المبيع. وقال بعضهم : لا يسقط. وهو الصحيح لأن المبيع لا يرى في ا الماء على حاله بل يرى أكبر مما كان فهذه الرؤية لا تعرف المبيع وإن كان المبيع مما يطعم فلا بد من الذوق لأنه المعرف المقصود، وإن كان مما يشم فلا بد من شمه كالمسك . وفي الولوالجية : اشترى نافجة مسك فأخرج المسك منها ليس له الرد بخيار الرؤية ولا بخيار العيب لأن الإخراج يدخل عليه عيباً ظاهراً حتى لو لم يدخل كان له أن يرد بخيار العيب والرؤية جميعاً اهـ. وفي جامع الفصولين: اشترى داراً واستثنى منه بيتاً معيناً لا بد من رؤية المستثنى فكما يشترط رؤية المبيع لسقوط الخيار يشترط رؤية المستثنى لأن جهالة وصف المستثنى توجب جهالة في المستثنى منه .اهـ. وقدمنا عن الخانية حكم ما إذا اشترى مغيباً في الأرض. وفي الظهيرية وفي الثمار على رؤوس الأشجار يعتبر رؤية جميعها بخلاف الموضوعة على الأرض، وفي تراب المعدن وتراب الصواغين يعتبر رؤية ما يخرج منه، ورؤية
As for cloth, the author (al-Musannif) sufficed with seeing it in a folded state, because the observer knows what is in the fold. If he required opening it, the seller would be harmed by its being broken and its value decreasing, and thereby its price would be reduced, unless it has two sides, in which case seeing both is necessary, or if there is something in the fold intended for sight like a pattern. Then it is said: this is according to their custom; as for our custom, unless the inside is seen, his option does not drop because the difference between inside and outside in clothes has become established; that is the view of Zufar. In al-Mabsut: the answer follows what Zufar said. In al-Zuhayriyya: seeing the outer covering suffices, unless the lining is intended, such as if it is of sable or similar, then its sight is considered. End quote. As for a house, in the apparent narration, if he sees its outside or sees the trees of the garden from outside, it suffices. According to Zufar, entering inside houses is necessary. The correct view is that the answer of the Book (al-Kitab) was in accordance with their custom in buildings, for their houses were not varied at that time. But today, entering inside the house is necessary due to variation; looking at the exterior does not give knowledge of the interior. In Jami' al-Fusulayn, this is the fatwa. So the conclusion is that the author (may Allah have mercy on him) chose Zufar's view regarding the house, and he should have chosen it regarding cloth, for the preferred view is Zufar's in both. Some scholars require seeing the upper floor, kitchen, and rubbish dump – that is the most apparent and similar (to the school?) as al-Shafi'i said, and it is the standard in the lands of Egypt and Syria. And the author did not mention the rest of the types of sold items, and we must mention them. They said: In a garden, seeing its exterior and interior is necessary. In a vineyard, seeing a sample of each type of grape is necessary. In pomegranates, seeing both sweet and sour is necessary. If one buys oil in a bottle, seeing it from outside the bottle is not sufficient until he pours it into his palm according to Abu Hanifa, because he has not actually seen the oil due to the barrier. In al-Tuhfa: if he looked into a mirror and saw the sold item, they said: his option does not drop because he saw not the very item but its reflection. If he bought fish in water that can be taken without hunting, and he saw it in the water, some said: his option drops because he saw the very item; some said: it does not drop. The correct view is that it does not drop, because the item is not seen in water as it is, but appears larger than it is; thus this sight does not inform one of the item. If the item is something to be tasted, tasting is necessary because it is the intended identifier; if it is something to be smelled, like musk, smelling is necessary. In al-Walwalijiyya: if one buys a musk pod and extracts the musk from it, he does not have the right to return it by option of sight nor by option of defect, because the extraction introduces an apparent defect. Even if he had not extracted, he could return it by both options. End quote. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: if one buys a house and excludes from it a specific room, seeing the excluded part is necessary. Just as seeing the sold item is required for the option to drop, so is seeing the excluded part required, because ignorance of the description of the excluded part causes ignorance in the part from which it is excluded. End quote. We have previously presented from al-Khaniyya the ruling of one who buys something buried in the ground. In al-Zuhayriyya: for fruits on the heads of trees, seeing all of them is considered, contrary to those placed on the ground. And in the soil of a mine and the soil of goldsmiths, seeing what comes out of it is considered. And sight...
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage continues the exposition of khiyar al-ru'ya, now focusing on further categories of sold items. For cloth, the early Hanafi position (based on custom) was that seeing the cloth folded sufficed, because the outer reveals the inner. However, as customs changed (differing inside and outside), the view of Imam Zufar gained traction: unless the buyer sees the inner side, the option does not drop. This is the mufta bihi (operative fatwa) today. For a house, similarly, the old custom allowed seeing the exterior or garden from outside to drop the option, but due to variability in modern buildings, entering inside is required. This is the fatwa according to Jami' al-Fusulayn. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq chose Zufar's view for houses, and the commentator says he should have done so for cloth as well. Some even require seeing specific parts like upper floor, kitchen, etc., which is the practice in Egypt and Syria. The passage then moves to other items: gardens require both exterior and interior; vineyards require seeing a sample of each grape type; pomegranates require seeing both sweet and sour; oil in a bottle must be poured out to be seen (Abu Hanifa); a mirror reflection does not count; fish in water is not reliably seen; items needing taste (food) or smell (musk) require those senses. A special case from al-Walwalijiyya: if a musk pod is opened and the musk extracted, the buyer cannot return it because the extraction itself is a change; if unopened, he can return by both options. Finally, an exception: if a room is excluded from a house sale, that room must be seen. The passage touches on buried items, fruits on trees vs on ground, and mineral soil.

Key Terms
ظهارة (zihara): The outer covering of a cloth or garment.
بطانة (bitana): The lining or inner side of a garment.
نافجة مسك (nafijat musk): A musk pod (the gland of a musk deer).
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of sight, i.e., the buyer's right to rescind a contract once the object is seen if it does not match the description or expectation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the mufta bihi (operative fatwa) for khiyar al-ru'ya when purchasing a house in modern times?
A) Seeing the exterior is sufficient.
B) The buyer must enter inside and see the interior.
C) Seeing the garden and exterior is enough.
D) The option does not apply to houses.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) can change the conditions for khiyar al-ru'ya; what suffices for sight evolves with the market and construction practices.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Rulings on Sight for Lapsing the Option of Seeing (Cloth, Houses, and Other Items)
Rulings on sufficient sight to lapse the option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when sight of a sold item lapses the option of seeing for cloth, houses, gardens, and other specific items, and explain how custom affects the ruling.

Core Idea
For cloth and houses, modern custom requires seeing inner parts or entering inside — the fatwa is with Zufar.

The option of seeing (khiyar al-ru'yah) allows a buyer who purchases an unseen item to annul the sale after seeing it if it does not meet expectations. However, the option is only removed by a sight that fully informs. This lesson explains what constitutes sufficient sight for different types of property, noting how the Hanafi school has adapted its rulings due to changes in custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of property
Determine whether the item is cloth, a house, a garden, oil in a bottle, a reflection, fish in water, or something involving taste/smell.
Each type has specific requirements.
2
Apply the default rule from the apparent narration
For cloth, seeing the outer side sufficed in early custom; for houses, seeing the exterior sufficed. For other items, apply the general principle that sight must inform fully.
Default rules are based on the custom of the time.
3
Check for intra-Hanafi disagreement and custom change
Note that Zufar required inner sight for cloth and entry into houses. The text indicates that custom has changed (inner/outer differences became common), so Zufar's view is now adopted.
This shows how custom (urf) can change the operative ruling.
4
Determine the fatwa today
For cloth and houses, the fatwa is with Zufar: inner sight or entry is required. For gardens, both outer and inner must be seen; for oil in a bottle, it must be poured out; for reflections, they do not suffice.
Fatwa follows the view that best prevents future disputes.

ورؤية المطوى وداخل الدار ونظر وكيله بالقبض كنظره لا نظر رسوله وصح عقد الأعمى وسقط قوله: (دون الصرم) الصرم الجلد قاموس قوله: (ونظر وكيله بالقبض كنظره لا نظر رسوله) أي بأن قبض الوكيل وهو ينظر إليه؛ كذا في البدائع. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا هما سواء وله الرد لأنه توكل بالقبض دون إسقاط الخيار فلا يملك ما لم يتوكل به وصار كخيار العيب والشرط والإسقاط قصداً. أوله أن القبض نوعان : تام وهو أن يقبضه وهو قصداً . بعد بخلاف خيار العيب لأنه لا يمنع تمام الصفقة فيتم القبض مع بقائه وخيار الشرط على الخلاف . ولو سلم فالموكل لا يملك التام منه فإنه لا يسقط بقبضه فإن الإختيار وهو المقصود بالخيار يكون بعده فكذا لا يملكه وكيله وبخلاف الرسول لأنه لا يملك شيئاً وإنما إليه تبليغ الرسالة ولهذا لا يملك القبض إذا كان رسولاً في البيع . قيد الوكيل بالقبض لأنه لو كان وكيلاً بالشراء فرؤيته مسقطة للخيار بالإجماع؛ كذا في الهداية ثم اعلم أنهم جعلوا الوكيل بالقبض كالرسول في مسائل منها لا يصح إبراؤه بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع، ومنها لا رجوع عليه بالثمن إذا رد المبيع بعيب بعد ما دفع إلى الموكل بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع، ومنها لو حلف لا يقبض فوكل به حنث بخلاف لا يبيع فوكل لا يحنث، ومنها تصح كفالة الوكيل بقبض الثمن المشتري بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع، ومنها قبول شهادة الوكيل بقبض الدين به، وستأتي المسائل في كتاب الوكالة تماماً إن شاء الله تعالى. وبهذا يترجح قولهما هنا أنه بمنزلة الرسول ورؤية الرسول بالشراء لا تسقط الخيار، كذا في المحيط. وفي المعراج : قيل الفرق بين الرسول والوكيل أن الوكيل لا يضيف العقد إلى الموكل والرسول لا يستغنى عن إضافته إلى المرسل وإليه الإشارة في قوله تعالى : يا أيها الرسول بلغ ﴾ [المائدة: ٦٧] وقوله تعالى وما أنت عليهم بوكيل [الأنعام: ۱۰۷] قل لست عليكم بوكيل [الأنعام: ٦٦] نفى
The chapter of seeing (in sales). Regarding seeing the udder: and 'kifl' with two fatha means the rump; as in al-Misbah. As for cloth, the author sufficed with its outer appearance folded because the beginner knows what is in the fold; if he required opening it, the seller would be harmed by its tearing and decrease in value, and thus its price would be reduced for him, unless it has two faces, then seeing both is necessary, or there is in its fold what is intended by sight, like a mark. Then it was said: this is in their custom; as for our custom, if he does not see the inner side, his option does not lapse because the difference between inner and outer in clothes is established, and this is the saying of Zufar. In al-Mabsut: the answer is as Zufar said. In al-Zahiriyya: seeing the outer side suffices unless the lining is intended, like if it is of sable or similar, then its seeing is considered. As for a house, the apparent narration is that if he sees its outside or sees the trees of the garden from outside, it suffices. According to Zufar, entering inside dwellings is necessary, and the correct view is that the answer of the book is in accordance with their custom in buildings, for their houses were not different at that time; but today, entering inside the house is necessary due to difference, for looking at the outside does not give knowledge of the inside. In Jami' al-Fusulayn, and by it fatwa is given. So the conclusion is that the author chose Zufar's saying regarding the house, and he should have chosen it regarding the cloth, for the chosen view is his saying in both. Some conditioned seeing the upper floor, kitchen, and garbage pit, and it is the most apparent and similar view, as al-Shafi'i said, and it is the relied-upon view in the lands of Egypt and Syria. The author did not mention the remaining types of sold items, but they must be mentioned. They said: in the garden, seeing its outer and inner is necessary; in the vineyard, seeing some grapes of each type; in pomegranates, seeing the sweet and sour. If one buys oil in a bottle, seeing it from outside the bottle is not sufficient until he pours it into his palm according to Abu Hanifa, because he did not actually see the oil due to the barrier. In al-Tuhfa: if he looks in a mirror and sees the sold item, they said: his option does not lapse because he saw its reflection, not its actual self. If he buys fish in water that can be taken without fishing, and he sees it in the water, some said: his option lapses because he saw the actual sold item. Others said: it does not lapse, and this is correct because the sold item is not seen in water as it is, but larger than it is; this sight does not identify the sold item. If the sold item is something tasted, then tasting is necessary because it is the intended identifier; if something smelled, then smelling is necessary, like musk. In al-Walwaljiyya: one bought a musk pod and took out the musk from it; he does not have the right of return by option of seeing or by option of defect, because taking it out introduces an apparent defect, so if he had not taken it out, he could return by both options. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: one bought a house and excepted a specific room; seeing the excepted part is necessary; just as seeing the sold item is required for lapse of option, seeing the excepted part is required because ignorance of the description of the excepted part causes ignorance in the item from which it is excepted. We have previously mentioned from al-Khaniyya the ruling on buying something buried in the earth. In al-Zahiriyya: regarding fruits on trees, seeing all of them is considered, unlike those placed on the ground; regarding ore soil and goldsmiths' soil, seeing what comes out of it is considered. And seeing the folded (cloth) and the inside of the house, and the sight of his agent in receipt is like his own sight, not the sight of his messenger, and the contract of a blind person is valid and his option lapses. His saying: (without the edge) the edge is the skin, from Qamus. His saying: (And the sight of his agent in receipt is like his own sight, not the sight of his messenger) meaning that the agent receives it while he (the agent) is looking at it; thus in al-Bada'i. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: both are the same, and he (the buyer) has the right of return because he was appointed for receipt, not for dropping the option, so he does not possess what he was not appointed for, and it becomes like the option of defect, condition, and intentional dropping. The first interpretation is that receipt is of two types: complete, which is that he receives it while seeing it; and incomplete, which is that he receives it covered. This is because its completion is with completion of the transaction, and it is not complete with the remaining of the option of seeing, and the principal owns it in both types, so too the agent due to the generality of his appointment. If he receives it covered, the appointment is completed with the incomplete type, so he cannot drop it intentionally. Unlike the option of defect because it does not prevent completion of the transaction, so receipt is complete with its remainder, and the option of condition is subject to disagreement. Even if agreed, the principal does not own the complete type from it, for it is not dropped by his receipt, for the choice which is the purpose of the option comes after it, so too his agent does not own it. And unlike the messenger because he owns nothing; it is only to convey the message, thus he does not own receipt if he is a messenger in the sale. He restricted (the agent) to the agent in receipt because if he were an agent in purchase, his sight would drop the option by consensus; thus in al-Hidayah. Then know that they made the agent in receipt like the messenger in certain issues: his discharge is not valid, unlike the agent in sale; he has no recourse for the price if the sold item is returned due to defect after he paid the principal, unlike the agent in sale; if one swears not to receive, and he appoints an agent, he breaks his oath, unlike if he swears not to sell and appoints an agent, he does not break his oath; the guarantee of the agent in receipt for the price from the buyer is valid, unlike the agent in sale; the testimony of the agent in receipt for the debt is accepted. These issues will come in the Book of Agency completely if God wills. And by this, their saying here that he is like the messenger is preferred, and the sight of the messenger in purchase does not drop the option, thus in al-Muhit. In al-Mi'raj: it was said the difference between the messenger and the agent is that the agent does not attribute the contract to the principal, while the messenger cannot dispense with attributing it to the sender, and this is indicated in His saying: 'O Messenger, convey' (Maidah 67) and His saying: 'And you are not over them a guardian' (An'am 107) and 'I am not over you a guardian' (An'am 66).
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a detailed commentary on the chapter of the Option of Seeing (Khayar al-Ru'yah). The author explains that the option granted to a buyer who purchases an item without seeing it is only removed when the buyer sees the item in a manner that fully informs him of its condition. The text first discusses the ruling for different types of goods. For cloth, the earlier custom allowed seeing only the outer appearance when folded, but later customs required seeing the inner side due to discrepancies between outer and inner quality. Imam Zufar's view that seeing both sides is necessary is adopted in our time. For houses, the original practice allowed seeing only the exterior, but due to changes in construction, entering inside is now required. The fatwa follows this. For gardens and vineyards, both outer and inner must be seen; for pomegranates, both sweet and sour varieties. Specific rulings are given for oil in a bottle (must be poured into the palm), reflections in a mirror (does not suffice), fish in water (does not suffice because water distorts size), and items involving taste or smell (must be tasted or smelled). The text then addresses the issue of an agent authorized to receive the sold item: if the agent sees the item while receiving it, the buyer's option lapses according to Abu Hanifa, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the agent's sight does not lapse the option unless the buyer himself sees it. The author sides with Abu Hanifa in principle but notes that in many practical aspects the agent in receipt is treated like a messenger (whose sight never lapses the option). The distinction is that a messenger merely conveys, while an agent acts on behalf of the principal. The passage ends by listing several consequences where the agent in receipt is likened to a messenger, such as in matters of discharge, recourse, and testimony.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The right of the buyer to annul the sale after seeing the purchased item if it does not conform to his expectation or if there is a defect that was not apparent at the time of contract.
ظهارة (zihara): The outer side or lining of a garment.
بطانة (bitana): The inner lining of a garment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling today for a buyer who purchases a house without seeing it inside and then only looks at the exterior?
A) His option lapses because the exterior sight is sufficient.
B) His option does not lapse because he must enter the house.
C) His option lapses only if he also sees the garden.
D) His option does not lapse regardless of interior sight.

Key Takeaway
The sufficiency of sight for khiyar al-ru'yah depends on custom and the nature of the item; for cloth and houses, the fatwa follows Zufar requiring inner sight/entry.

The Dispute on the Agent in Receipt and Messenger: Does Their Sight Lapse the Option?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent in receipt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the sight of an agent in receipt (wakil bil-qabd) lapses the buyer's option of seeing, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: agent's sight lapses option; Sahibayn: agent is like a messenger and does not.

When a buyer appoints an agent to receive a purchased item, does the agent's sight of the item cause the buyer's option of seeing to lapse? Or is the agent like a messenger whose sight has no effect? This lesson unpacks the detailed reasoning of Abu Hanifa versus Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with the practical consequences and the preferred fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the two positions
Abu Hanifa holds that if the agent in receipt sees the item while receiving it, the buyer's khiyar al-ru'yah lapses. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that it does not; the agent is like a messenger.
The key distinction is complete vs. incomplete receipt.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Receipt (qabd) is of two types: complete (with sight) and incomplete (without sight). The agent is appointed for receipt in general, so he can complete the receipt and thereby perfect the transaction, lapsing the option.
The principal owns both types of receipt, and so does the agent by general appointment.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's reasoning
The agent was appointed only for receipt, not for dropping the option. Dropping the option is a separate right. Also, the agent in receipt is likened to a messenger in many rulings, and a messenger's sight never lapses the option.
They analogize to khiyar al-'ayb (defect) where the option is not dropped by receipt.
4
Analyze the tarjih
The text lists several practical consequences where the agent in receipt is treated like a messenger (e.g., discharge, recourse, oath-breaking). By this, the Sahibayn's view is preferred in those contexts, implying that their view on the option is also strong.
However, the text does not explicitly state which view is mufta bihi for the option itself.
5
Determine the practical effect
If the buyer sends an agent who sees the item, according to Abu Hanifa the buyer loses the option; according to the Sahibayn, the buyer retains it. In practice, the fatwa tends to follow the Sahibayn's view in many matters, but caution is advised.
The Quranic verses on messenger vs. guardian are cited to support the linguistic distinction.

.اهـ. وقدمنا عن الخانية حكم ما إذا اشترى مغيباً في الأرض. وفي الظهيرية وفي الثمار على رؤوس الأشجار يعتبر رؤية جميعها بخلاف الموضوعة على الأرض، وفي تراب المعدن وتراب الصواغين يعتبر رؤية ما يخرج منه، ورؤية المطوى وداخل الدار ونظر وكيله بالقبض كنظره لا نظر رسوله وصح عقد الأعمى وسقط قوله: (دون الصرم) الصرم الجلد قاموس قوله: (ونظر وكيله بالقبض كنظره لا نظر رسوله) أي بأن قبض الوكيل وهو ينظر إليه؛ كذا في البدائع. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا هما سواء وله الرد لأنه توكل بالقبض دون إسقاط الخيار فلا يملك ما لم يتوكل به وصار كخيار العيب والشرط والإسقاط قصداً. أوله أن القبض نوعان : تام وهو أن يقبضه وهو قصداً . بعد بخلاف خيار العيب لأنه لا يمنع تمام الصفقة فيتم القبض مع بقائه وخيار الشرط على الخلاف . ولو سلم فالموكل لا يملك التام منه فإنه لا يسقط بقبضه فإن الإختيار وهو المقصود بالخيار يكون بعده فكذا الهداية ثم اعلم أنهم جعلوا الوكيل بالقبض كالرسول في مسائل منها لا يصح إبراؤه بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع، ومنها لا رجوع عليه بالثمن إذا رد المبيع بعيب بعد ما دفع إلى الموكل بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع، ومنها لو حلف إضافته إلى المرسل وإليه الإشارة في قوله تعالى : يا أيها الرسول بلغ ﴾ [المائدة: ٦٧] وقوله تعالى وما أنت عليهم بوكيل [الأنعام: ۱۰۷] قل لست عليكم بوكيل [الأنعام: ٦٦] نفى
The chapter of seeing (in sales). Regarding seeing the udder: and 'kifl' with two fatha means the rump; as in al-Misbah. As for cloth, the author sufficed with its outer appearance folded because the beginner knows what is in the fold; if he required opening it, the seller would be harmed by its tearing and decrease in value, and thus its price would be reduced for him, unless it has two faces, then seeing both is necessary, or there is in its fold what is intended by sight, like a mark. Then it was said: this is in their custom; as for our custom, if he does not see the inner side, his option does not lapse because the difference between inner and outer in clothes is established, and this is the saying of Zufar. In al-Mabsut: the answer is as Zufar said. In al-Zahiriyya: seeing the outer side suffices unless the lining is intended, like if it is of sable or similar, then its seeing is considered. As for a house, the apparent narration is that if he sees its outside or sees the trees of the garden from outside, it suffices. According to Zufar, entering inside dwellings is necessary, and the correct view is that the answer of the book is in accordance with their custom in buildings, for their houses were not different at that time; but today, entering inside the house is necessary due to difference, for looking at the outside does not give knowledge of the inside. In Jami' al-Fusulayn, and by it fatwa is given. So the conclusion is that the author chose Zufar's saying regarding the house, and he should have chosen it regarding the cloth, for the chosen view is his saying in both. Some conditioned seeing the upper floor, kitchen, and garbage pit, and it is the most apparent and similar view, as al-Shafi'i said, and it is the relied-upon view in the lands of Egypt and Syria. The author did not mention the remaining types of sold items, but they must be mentioned. They said: in the garden, seeing its outer and inner is necessary; in the vineyard, seeing some grapes of each type; in pomegranates, seeing the sweet and sour. If one buys oil in a bottle, seeing it from outside the bottle is not sufficient until he pours it into his palm according to Abu Hanifa, because he did not actually see the oil due to the barrier. In al-Tuhfa: if he looks in a mirror and sees the sold item, they said: his option does not lapse because he saw its reflection, not its actual self. If he buys fish in water that can be taken without fishing, and he sees it in the water, some said: his option lapses because he saw the actual sold item. Others said: it does not lapse, and this is correct because the sold item is not seen in water as it is, but larger than it is; this sight does not identify the sold item. If the sold item is something tasted, then tasting is necessary because it is the intended identifier; if something smelled, then smelling is necessary, like musk. In al-Walwaljiyya: one bought a musk pod and took out the musk from it; he does not have the right of return by option of seeing or by option of defect, because taking it out introduces an apparent defect, so if he had not taken it out, he could return by both options. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: one bought a house and excepted a specific room; seeing the excepted part is necessary; just as seeing the sold item is required for lapse of option, seeing the excepted part is required because ignorance of the description of the excepted part causes ignorance in the item from which it is excepted. We have previously mentioned from al-Khaniyya the ruling on buying something buried in the earth. In al-Zahiriyya: regarding fruits on trees, seeing all of them is considered, unlike those placed on the ground; regarding ore soil and goldsmiths' soil, seeing what comes out of it is considered. And seeing the folded (cloth) and the inside of the house, and the sight of his agent in receipt is like his own sight, not the sight of his messenger, and the contract of a blind person is valid and his option lapses. His saying: (without the edge) the edge is the skin, from Qamus. His saying: (And the sight of his agent in receipt is like his own sight, not the sight of his messenger) meaning that the agent receives it while he (the agent) is looking at it; thus in al-Bada'i. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: both are the same, and he (the buyer) has the right of return because he was appointed for receipt, not for dropping the option, so he does not possess what he was not appointed for, and it becomes like the option of defect, condition, and intentional dropping. The first interpretation is that receipt is of two types: complete, which is that he receives it while seeing it; and incomplete, which is that he receives it covered. This is because its completion is with completion of the transaction, and it is not complete with the remaining of the option of seeing, and the principal owns it in both types, so too the agent due to the generality of his appointment. If he receives it covered, the appointment is completed with the incomplete type, so he cannot drop it intentionally. Unlike the option of defect because it does not prevent completion of the transaction, so receipt is complete with its remainder, and the option of condition is subject to disagreement. Even if agreed, the principal does not own the complete type from it, for it is not dropped by his receipt, for the choice which is the purpose of the option comes after it, so too his agent does not own it. And unlike the messenger because he owns nothing; it is only to convey the message, thus he does not own receipt if he is a messenger in the sale. He restricted (the agent) to the agent in receipt because if he were an agent in purchase, his sight would drop the option by consensus; thus in al-Hidayah. Then know that they made the agent in receipt like the messenger in certain issues: his discharge is not valid, unlike the agent in sale; he has no recourse for the price if the sold item is returned due to defect after he paid the principal, unlike the agent in sale; if one swears not to receive, and he appoints an agent, he breaks his oath, unlike if he swears not to sell and appoints an agent, he does not break his oath; the guarantee of the agent in receipt for the price from the buyer is valid, unlike the agent in sale; the testimony of the agent in receipt for the debt is accepted. These issues will come in the Book of Agency completely if God wills. And by this, their saying here that he is like the messenger is preferred, and the sight of the messenger in purchase does not drop the option, thus in al-Muhit. In al-Mi'raj: it was said the difference between the messenger and the agent is that the agent does not attribute the contract to the principal, while the messenger cannot dispense with attributing it to the sender, and this is indicated in His saying: 'O Messenger, convey' (Maidah 67) and His saying: 'And you are not over them a guardian' (An'am 107) and 'I am not over you a guardian' (An'am 66).
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a detailed commentary on the chapter of the Option of Seeing (Khayar al-Ru'yah). The author explains that the option granted to a buyer who purchases an item without seeing it is only removed when the buyer sees the item in a manner that fully informs him of its condition. The text first discusses the ruling for different types of goods. For cloth, the earlier custom allowed seeing only the outer appearance when folded, but later customs required seeing the inner side due to discrepancies between outer and inner quality. Imam Zufar's view that seeing both sides is necessary is adopted in our time. For houses, the original practice allowed seeing only the exterior, but due to changes in construction, entering inside is now required. The fatwa follows this. For gardens and vineyards, both outer and inner must be seen; for pomegranates, both sweet and sour varieties. Specific rulings are given for oil in a bottle (must be poured into the palm), reflections in a mirror (does not suffice), fish in water (does not suffice because water distorts size), and items involving taste or smell (must be tasted or smelled). The text then addresses the issue of an agent authorized to receive the sold item: if the agent sees the item while receiving it, the buyer's option lapses according to Abu Hanifa, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the agent's sight does not lapse the option unless the buyer himself sees it. The author sides with Abu Hanifa in principle but notes that in many practical aspects the agent in receipt is treated like a messenger (whose sight never lapses the option). The distinction is that a messenger merely conveys, while an agent acts on behalf of the principal. The passage ends by listing several consequences where the agent in receipt is likened to a messenger, such as in matters of discharge, recourse, and testimony.

Key Terms
وكيل بالقبض (wakil bil-qabd): An agent appointed by the buyer to take receipt of the purchased item on his behalf.
رسول (rasul): A messenger who merely conveys communication or items without independent authority to act on behalf of the principal.
قبض تام (qabd tam): Complete receipt that perfects the transaction, achieved when the receiver sees the item while taking possession.
قبض ناقص (qabd naqis): Incomplete receipt where the item is taken without seeing it, leaving the transaction and option incomplete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the distinguishing feature between a messenger and an agent in receipt that affects the option of seeing?
A) A messenger can return the item but an agent cannot.
B) A messenger does not own the receipt while an agent can perform complete receipt.
C) An agent must be a relative of the buyer.
D) A messenger's sight is more reliable than an agent's.

Key Takeaway
The sight of an agent in receipt may or may not lapse khiyar al-ru'yah depending on whether one follows Abu Hanifa (lapses) or the Sahibayn (does not), with the latter preferred in many practical rulings.

What Sight is Sufficient to Waive the Option of Sight
Rulings on what sight is sufficient to waive option of sight, with expansions from commentaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for sufficient sight that waive the option of sight and apply the principle of 'taba' (subsidiary vs original) to determine whether a buyer retains the option.

Core Idea
Seeing only a part does not waive the option unless it reveals the essential nature of the item.

When you buy something unseen, you have the right to rescind after seeing it. But what counts as 'seeing'? This lesson, drawn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentaries, clarifies which types of sight are legally sufficient to end that right. We explore partial sight, items with two faces, and the crucial distinction between subsidiary and original components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Understand that the act of sight by the buyer can waive the khiyar al-ru'yah. The general rule is that the option remains until the item is seen sufficiently.
Khiyar al-ru'yah is a right, not an obligation.
2
Apply examples of insufficient sight
From the matn: seeing one panel of a double door, one slipper, one shoe, or only the back of a carpet does not waive the option. List each case and why it fails.
These examples show that partial or one-sided sight is insufficient.
3
Distinguish items with two faces
If an item has two distinct faces (like front and back of a rug), both must be seen. The commentary adds that for a rug, all parts must be seen.
Two-faced items require both faces to be viewed.
4
Analyze the taba' principle from al-Muhit
If the unseen part is subsidiary (taba') to the seen, the option lapses. If unseen is original, check if the seen part informs its condition: if yes, option lapses; if no, remains.
This principle resolves complex items like stuffed pillows.
5
Apply to modern variations
Consider the author's adjustment for the slave-girl's hand (صرم) in later times, and for stuffed pillows with customary vs non-customary stuffing.
Custom and intention matter in determining sufficient sight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب خيار الرؤية المطوى وداخل الدار ونظر وكيله بالقبض كنظره لا نظر رسوله وصح عقد الأعمى وسقط . أحد المصراعين أو أحد الخفين أو أحد النعلين لا يكفي ولا يكفي أن يرى ظاهر الطنفسة ما لم ير وجهها وموضع الشيء منها وما كان له وجهان مختلفان تعتبر رؤيتهما اهـ. وفي المعراج : وفي البساط لا بد من رؤية جميعه، ولو نظر إلى ظهور المكاعب لا يبطل خياره، ولو نظر إلى وجهها دون الصرم يبطل قلت وينبغي أن يشترط رؤية الصرم في زماننا لتفاوته وكونه مقصوداً. وفي الوسادة المحشوة لو رأى ظاهرها فإن كانت محشوة مما يحشى مثلها يبطل خياره، وإن كان مما لا يحشى مثلها فله الخيار اهـ. وفي المحيط الأصل أن غير المرئي إن كان تبعاً للمرئي فلا خيار له في غير المرئي، وإن كان غير المرئي أصلاً فإن كان رؤية ما رأى لم تعرفه حال رؤيته بقي خياره وإن كانت تعرفه بطل اهـ.
Book of Sales, Chapter of the Option of Sight: The folded and the interior of a house, and the sight of his agent in receiving is like his sight, not the sight of his messenger, and the contract of a blind person is valid and the option drops. One panel of a double-door, or one slipper or one shoe is not sufficient, and it is not sufficient to see the back of a carpet unless he sees its face and the place of the thing from it. Whatever has two different faces, seeing both is required. End. And in al-Mi'raj: For a rug, it is necessary to see all of it. If he looked at the back of a slave-girl, his option does not lapse; if he looked at her face without the back of the hand, it lapses. I said: It should be required to see the back of the hand in our time because of its variation and being intended. And regarding a stuffed pillow, if he saw its outer appearance, if it is stuffed with something that is customarily stuffed in it, his option lapses; if it is with something not customarily stuffed in it, he has the option. End. And in al-Muhit: The principle is that if the unseen is subsidiary to the seen, then he has no option regarding the unseen; if the unseen is the original, then if seeing what he saw does not inform him of its condition at the time of seeing, his option remains; but if it informs him, it lapses. End.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentaries regarding what constitutes sufficient sight to waive the option of sight (خيار الرؤية) in a sale. The chapter begins by stating that if a person buys an item sight unseen, he has the option to confirm or rescind upon seeing it. The matn lists examples where partial sight is insufficient: seeing one panel of a double door, one slipper, one shoe, or only the back of a carpet without its face does not waive the right. For an item with two different faces (e.g., front and back), both must be seen. The commentary from al-Mi'raj adds that for a rug, the entire rug must be seen. For a slave-girl, seeing her back does not waive the option, but seeing her face without the back of the hand (صرم) does waive it. The author notes that in later times, seeing the back of the hand should be required due to its variation and desirability. For a stuffed pillow, if the stuffing is customary, seeing the outside waives the option; if not, the option remains. Finally, al-Muhit states a general principle: if the unseen part is subsidiary to the seen part (i.e., follows it in purpose), then no option remains for the unseen; but if the unseen is the original element (like the stuffing in a pillow), then if the seen part indicates its condition, the option lapses; otherwise it remains. These rules help determine when a buyer who has not seen an item fully still retains the right to reject it.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of sight: a right a buyer has to rescind a contract after seeing an item that was purchased unseen.
تبع (taba'): A subsidiary part that follows the main part in purpose and value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a folded carpet without seeing it. He later sees only the back of the carpet. Does he still have the option of sight?
A) Yes, because seeing only the back is not sufficient.
B) No, because seeing any part of the carpet waives the option.
C) Yes, but only if the carpet has a single face.
D) No, because the carpet was not seen at all.

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-ru'yah, sufficient sight requires seeing the essential aspect(s) of the item; partial or one-sided sight does not waive the option unless the unseen is subsidiary and indicated by the seen.

Does the Agent's Sight Waive the Option of Sight?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's sight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi dispute on whether an agent for receipt's sight waives the option of sight, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
An agent for receipt does not waive the option of sight—he is like a messenger, not the buyer.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks a key disagreement within the Hanafi school. Imam Abu Hanifa held that an agent who receives a purchased item and sees it acts just as the buyer would: the option of sight drops. His two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, disagreed: the agent was appointed only to receive, not to drop options, so his sight has no effect. They fortified their view by listing five cases where the agent for receipt is treated exactly like a messenger. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish agency roles and apply the correct fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles
The buyer appoints an agent specifically to receive the purchased item (وكيل بالقبض). Distinguish this from an agent for purchase. Also note the messenger (رسول) who merely conveys.
The agent for receipt is not the buyer; his authority is limited to taking physical possession.
2
State the dispute
Abu Hanifa says the agent's sight is like the buyer's sight, so the option drops. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say his sight does not drop the option.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Receipt is two types: complete (with sight) and incomplete (without sight). The agent's authority is absolute, so he can complete the receipt. Once receipt is complete, the option ends. The buyer owns both types, so the agent inherits that authority.
Key concept: qabd tamm ends the option.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
The agent was appointed only for receipt, not to drop options. He cannot exercise a right he was not given. Also, in five rulings (release, recourse, oath, guarantee, testimony) the agent for receipt is treated like a messenger, who cannot waive the option.
The five similarities strengthen the analogy to a messenger.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The later authorities (e.g., al-Marghinani) favor the Sahibayn's view: the agent's sight does not drop the option. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
The exception is an agent for purchase, whose sight drops the option by consensus.

دل على إنابة المأمور مناب الأمر فراجعه قوله فله خيار الرؤية ولو لم يره) الذي من
That is his statement: '(And the sight of his agent in receipt is like his sight, not the sight of his messenger)' i.e., when the agent takes receipt while looking at it; thus in al-Bada'i. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two said they are the same, and he has the right to reject because he was appointed for receipt, not for dropping the option, so he does not possess what he was not appointed for, and it becomes like the option of defect and condition and intentional dropping. The other response: Receipt is of two types: complete—that he takes receipt while seeing it; and incomplete—that he takes it covered. This is because its completion is with the completion of the transaction, and it is not complete while the option of sight remains, and the principal owns it in both types, so too the agent because his appointment is absolute. If he takes it covered, the appointment ends with the incomplete type, so he does not have the ability to drop it intentionally afterwards. Unlike the option of defect because it does not prevent completion of the transaction, so receipt is complete while it remains; and the option of condition is subject to disagreement. Even if granted, the principal does not own the complete type, for it does not drop by his receipt, because choice (which is the purpose of the option) comes after it, so too his agent does not own it. And unlike the messenger because he owns nothing; his role is only to deliver the message, hence he does not own receipt if he is a messenger in the sale. The agent is restricted to receipt because if he were an agent to purchase, his sight drops the option by consensus; thus in al-Hidayah. Then know that they have made the agent for receipt like the messenger in some issues: 1. His release (of liability) is not valid, unlike the agent for sale. 2. There is no recourse against him for the price if he returns the sold item due to a defect after handing it to the principal, unlike the agent for sale. 3. If one swears not to receive, and appoints an agent, he breaks his oath, unlike if he swears not to sell and appoints an agent, he does not break. 4. The guarantee of the agent for receipt of the price from the buyer is valid, unlike the agent for sale. 5. The testimony of the agent for receipt of debt is accepted. These issues will come in the Book of Agency in full if Allah wills. And by this the position of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is strengthened here that he is like a messenger, and the sight of a messenger in purchase does not drop the option; thus in al-Muhit. And in al-Mi'raj: It was said the difference between the messenger and the agent is that the agent does not attribute the contract to the principal, and the messenger cannot do without attributing it to the sender. And to this refers Allah's saying: 'O Messenger, convey' (5:67) and His saying: 'And you are not a guardian (وكيل) over them' (6:107) and 'Say: I am not over you a guardian' (6:66) negated agency and confirmed being a messenger. In al-Fawa'id: The form of appointment (توكيل) is that the buyer says to another: 'Be an agent in receiving the sold item' or 'I appoint you to receive it'; and the form of sending (رسالة) is that he says: 'Be a messenger from me in receiving it' or 'I command you to receive it' or 'I send you to take it,' or he says: 'Tell so-and-so to hand the sold item to you.' And it was said: There is no difference between messenger and agent in the absolute command if he says: 'Receive the sold item,' then the option does not drop. End. And there is a deficiency in the Imam's statement that the agent is like the principal in two issues where the agent does not stand in the place of the principal: 1. If the agent sees before receipt, his sight does not drop the option; but if the principal sees and does not receive, his option drops. 2. If the principal receives it covered, then sees it, then after receipt he nullifies the option, it is nullified; if the agent does that, it is not nullified. And it was answered that the dropping of the option by the agent's receipt is established implicitly because his receipt is complete due to his authority by agency, and this is not established in mere sight before receipt. And we say: Rather the mentioned ruling for the principal—that his option drops if he sees it—only holds on the view that mere passage of what enables him to rescind after sight drops the option, and that is not correct; and with the same first answer the difference occurs in the second issue; thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Zahiriya: It is not valid to appoint an agent to drop the option of sight. End. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: Appointing an agent for sight intentionally is not valid, and his sight does not become the sight of his principal; so if he buys something unseen and appoints a man to see it and says: 'If you are pleased with it, take it,' it is not valid. And an agent for purchase, if he buys what his principal has seen without the agent knowing, he has the option of sight even if he did not see it. This is when he appointed him to buy an unspecified thing; for a specified thing, the agent has no option of sight. If he appointed him to buy a slave unspecified, and he buys a slave that the agent has seen, then neither he nor his principal has any option. Or meaning 'on behalf of', otherwise the one guaranteed to receive the price is the seller. His statement: '(And in al-Fawa'id etc.)' does not contradict what came before because that is about the difference between messenger and agent, and this is a difference between appointment (توكيل) and sending (إرسال), i.e., what makes someone an agent and what makes someone a messenger in terms of wording. The gist of the difference between the first two is that the agent is an immediate actor, the messenger is a deliverer; and this will come in the Book of Agency from Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: The agent is one who directly executes the contract, the messenger is one who delivers the direct execution. The gist of the difference between the second two is that the agent becomes an agent by words of agency, and the messenger becomes a messenger by words of sending; and by an absolute command, the command is a sending not an agency. This is contradicted by what will come in Agency from al-Bada'i' that the offer from the principal is to say: 'I appoint you for such-and-such' or 'Do such-and-such' or 'I permit you to do such-and-such' and similar. And the author said there: If you say: What is the difference between appointment and sending, since permission and command are appointment as you know? I say: The messenger is when he says to him: 'I sent you' or 'Be a messenger from me about such-and-such'; and al-Zayla'i in the Chapter of Option of Sight included 'I command you to receive it' among that. And he explicitly stated in al-Nihaya, attributing to al-Fawa'id al-Zuhayriya, that it is appointment, which agrees with what is in al-Bada'i', because there is no difference between 'Do such-and-such' and 'I command you to do such-and-such.' End. I say: What is transmitted here from al-Fawa'id is that a command is a sending, not an appointment: consider. But the author will mention in Agency from al-Walwalijiya what indicates that a command is an appointment if it indicates deputizing the commanded person to stand in place of the commander; so refer to it. His statement: 'he has the option of sight even if he did not see it' which is from...
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn line: 'The sight of his agent in receipt is like his sight, not the sight of his messenger.' It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the agent's sight when he receives the item is equivalent to the principal's sight, so the option of sight drops. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the agent and messenger are the same: neither's sight drops the option because the agent was only appointed for receipt, not for dropping the option. The commentary then provides a reasoning for Abu Hanifa's position: receipt is of two types—complete (when the agent sees the item) and incomplete (when he receives it unseen). The agent has authority to complete the receipt by seeing, and the principal owns both types, so the agent's sight completes the receipt and thereby drops the option. It then explains why the messenger is different: a messenger owns nothing and only conveys, so his sight has no effect. The commentary further notes that the agent's sight drops the option only if he is an agent for receipt; if he is an agent for purchase, his sight drops the option by consensus. Interestingly, the author then lists five issues where the agent for receipt is treated like a messenger (e.g., his release is invalid, no recourse for price), which strengthens the Sahibayn's view that the agent is like a messenger. The discussion continues with the wording distinctions between appointing an agent (توكيل) and sending a messenger (إرسال), citing various sources. The passage ends with rulings on appointing someone specifically for sight, showing that such an appointment is invalid, and clarifying cases where the agent's sight or ignorance affects the option. The overall legal conclusion is that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn: the agent for receipt is like a messenger, and his sight does not drop the option, except when he is an agent for purchase.

Key Terms
وكيل بالقبض (wakil bi al-qabd): An agent appointed specifically to take receipt of a purchased item.
رسول (rasul): A messenger who conveys a message or performs a task but does not act on his own authority; he owns nothing.
قبض تام (qabd tamm): Complete receipt that occurs when the receiver sees the item and takes it into his possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school, if a buyer appoints an agent to receive a purchased item, and the agent sees the item before receiving it, what effect does that have on the buyer's option of sight?
A) The option drops because the agent's sight is like the buyer's sight.
B) The option does not drop because the agent is like a messenger.
C) The option drops only if the agent was also the one who purchased.
D) The option does not drop unless the agent explicitly says he is satisfied.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi holds that an agent for receipt is like a messenger; his sight does not drop the option of sight—the buyer must see the item himself to lose the right to reject.

intermediate
11 min
The Agent vs. the Messenger in Khiyar al-Ru'yah
Explaining the distinction between agent and messenger in khiyar al-ru'yah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effect of an agent's sight from a messenger's sight before possession in khiyar al-ru'yah and state the Hanafi ruling with its rational proof.

Core Idea
An agent's sight before possession does NOT void khiyar al-ru'yah; only the principal's own sight does.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a crucial nuance in the Hanafi law of sale: Who must see an item to lose the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah)? The answer turns on whether the person seeing is acting as an agent (wakil) or a messenger (rasul). Understanding this distinction prevents a common error that could deprive a buyer of their right to rescind.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage addresses whether khiyar al-ru'yah falls when an agent (wakil) sees the sold item before taking possession, compared to when the principal (muwakkil) sees it.
Khiyar al-ru'yah is the buyer's right to cancel a contract upon seeing an item bought unseen.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi ruling is that the agent's sight does NOT void the option; only the principal's own sight before possession has that effect. The messenger's sight similarly does not affect the option.
This is the mufta bihi position as per Fath al-Qadir.
3
Locate the dalil (rational proof)
The proof is based on the nature of agency (wikalah). The agent's authority (wilaya) is limited to taking possession. The fall of khiyar by possession is incidental to that authority, not due to sight. Mere sight by the agent does not activate the same legal effect as the principal's sight.
The argument is aqli (rational) and supported by analysis of causality.
4
Address the counter-argument from Imam Abu Hanifa's statement
Imam Abu Hanifa's general statement that 'the agent is like the principal except in two issues' could imply that in other matters (including khiyar by sight) the agent stands in the principal's place. The passage argues this statement is deficient because the fall of khiyar by the principal's sight is itself a contested ruling.
The two exceptions are not exceptions but corrections to the general rule.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the agent's sight does not fall the option. This is supported by the analysis in Fath al-Qadir and the majority of later Hanafi authorities.
The praktical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that if an agent sees before possession, the buyer retains the option upon his own later sight.

Source Text

الوكالة وأثبت الرسالة. وفي الفوائد : صورة التوكيل أن يقول المشتري لغيره كن وكيلاً في قبض المبيع أو وكلتك بقبضة، وصورة الرسول أن يقول كن رسولاً عني في قبضة أو أمرتك بقبضة أو أرسلتك لتقبضه، أو قال قل Agency and the establishment of messengership. In al-Fawa'id: The form of agency is that the buyer says to another 'Be an agent for me in taking possession of the sold item' or 'I appointed you as agent for taking possession'; and the form of messengership is that he says 'Be a messenger for me in taking possession' or 'I commanded you to take possession' or 'I sent you to take possession', or he says 'Tell so-and-so to hand over the sold item to you.' And it was said there is no difference between the messenger and the agent in the matter of command, if he says 'Take possession of the sold item' so the option does not fall. End. And the statement of the Imam is deficient in two issues where the agent does not stand in place of the principal: one is that if the agent saw before possession, his sight does not cause the option to fall, while if the principal saw and did not take possession, his option falls. The second is that if the principal took possession while it was covered, then he saw it, then after possession he voided the option, it is void; but if the agent did that, it is not void. And it was answered that the falling of the option by the agent's possession is established incidentally due to the completion of his possession because of his authority by agency, and this is not established by mere sight before possession. And we say: Rather, the mentioned ruling for the principal—that his option falls when he sees it—is only valid on the view that mere passage of time in which he is able to rescind after sight falls the option, and that is not correct. And by the same first answer, the difference occurs in the second issue. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Zahiriyyah: It is not permitted to appoint an agent for the fall of khiyar al-ru'yah. End. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: Appointing an agent for the purpose of seeing is not [valid], and his sight does not become the principal's sight, so even if he bought something he had not seen and appointed a man for seeing it and said 'If you approve of it, take it', it is not valid. And an agent for buying, if he bought what his principal had seen and the agent did not know about it, he has khiyar al-ru'yah even if he did not see it. And this is when he appointed him to buy an unspecified thing; for a specified thing, the agent has no khiyar al-ru'yah. And if he appointed him to buy a slave without specification, and he bought a slave that the agent had seen, then neither he nor his principal has an option. Or in the sense of 'on behalf of', otherwise the guarantor of the price is the seller. His saying: (And in al-Fawa'id, etc.) This does not contradict what came before because that is about the difference between messenger and agent, and this is the difference between appointment as agent and appointment as messenger, i.e., how one becomes an agent and how one becomes a messenger. The words. And the summary of the difference between the first two is that the agent is a direct doer and the messenger is a conveyer, and this will come in the book of agency from Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: The agent is one who conducts the contract directly, the messenger is one who conveys the conduct. And the summary of the difference between the second two is that the agent becomes agent by words of agency, and the messenger becomes messenger by words of messengership, and by an absolute command, the command is messengership not agency. And this contradicts what will come in agency from al-Bada'i that the offer from the principal is to say 'I appointed you as agent for such-and-such' or 'Do such-and-such' or 'I permitted you to do such-and-such' and similar. And the author said there: If you say, what is the difference between agency and messengership, since permission and command are agency as you know? I say: The messenger is that he says to him 'I sent you' or 'Be a messenger for me in such-and-such', and al-Zayla'i included in the chapter of khiyar al-ru'yah 'I commanded you to take possession'. And it is explicitly stated in al-Nihayah, citing al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah, that it is agency, which agrees with al-Bada'i, for there is no difference between 'Do such-and-such' and 'I commanded you to do such-and-such'. End. I say: What is transmitted here from al-Fawa'id is that command is messengership, not agency. Deliberate. But the author will mention in agency from al-Walwaljiah what indicates that command is agency if it indicates deputization of the commanded person in place of the commander. So refer to it. His saying: 'Then he has khiyar al-ru'yah even if he did not see it.' Which is from
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the distinction between a wakil (agent) and a rasul (messenger) in the context of khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight). The author first presents the wording for appointing each: an agent is appointed with words like 'wakaltuka' (I appoint you as agent), while a messenger is appointed with words like 'arsaltuka' (I send you) or 'amartuka' (I command you). The key ruling is that if an agent sees the sold item before taking possession, his sight does not fall the khiyar; but if the principal himself sees before possession, his khiyar falls. This is based on the principle that the agent's authority is limited to the act of possession, and the khiyar falling by possession is incidental to his deputized role, not directly from sight. The text then addresses a statement of Imam Abu Hanifa that the agent is like the principal except in two issues; the commentary argues this statement is deficient because the fall of khiyar by the principal's sight is itself not the correct ruling (since the correct view is that mere ability to rescind after sight does not fall the option). The passage also discusses the validity of appointing an agent solely for the purpose of seeing (tawkil bi al-ru'yah), noting that it is not valid unless the agent is also given authority to rescind or ratify. Finally, it delves into the distinction between agency and messengership in general, noting that a command (amr) is considered messengership, not agency, according to some sources, but there is a difference of opinion.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakalah): The act of appointing someone as an agent to act on one's behalf; here specifically to take possession of the sold item.
الرسالة (al-risalah): The act of appointing someone as a messenger to convey a command or message, without independent discretion.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option for a buyer who purchases an unseen item to rescind the contract upon seeing it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer appoints an agent to take possession of a sold item, and the agent sees the item before taking possession, does the buyer's khiyar al-ru'yah fall?
A) Yes, the agent's sight is like the principal's sight.
B) No, the buyer still has the option when he sees it later.
C) Yes, but only if the agent also had authority to see on the buyer's behalf.
D) No, unless the agent also takes possession after sight.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the agent's sight before possession does not extinguish khiyar al-ru'yah; only the principal's own sight does.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Validity of Blind Person's Contracts and Their Khiyar al-Ru'yah
Stating validity of blind person's contracts and means of khiyar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a blind person's contracts and identify the means by which a blind buyer exercises khiyar al-ru'yah.

Core Idea
A blind person's contracts are valid; his khiyar al-ru'yah falls through touch, smell, taste, or description.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq confirms the general legal capacity of the blind in contracts, challenging the assumption that blindness disqualifies one from entering into valid sales. It then details how a blind buyer can exercise the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) through means that substitute for sight, with an important distinction between real estate and other items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
Read the first sentence: the contract of a blind person is valid (صح عقد الأعمى). Note the reason: he is a responsible person (مكلف) in need of contracts, like a sighted person.
This ruling applies to all contracts, not just sale.
2
Analyze the proof: practice akin to ijma
The author cites the continuous practice of people dealing with the blind without disapproval, which is treated as equivalent to consensus (بمنزلة الإجماع). This is an istidlal from 'urf and tacit approval.
The proof is zanni al-thubut but widely accepted.
3
List the exceptions where blind is not like sighted
The passage lists several issues where the blind person is treated differently: no jihad, Friday prayer, congregation for Hajj; cannot be witness; no full diyah for eyes; disliked adhan/imamate unless most knowledgeable; etc.
These exceptions do not affect the validity of contracts.
4
Understand khiyar al-ru'yah for the blind
Focus on the means: touching, smelling, tasting for movable items; description (وصف) for real estate. The option falls (يسقط) only if these occur before the purchase. If done after, they indicate approval instead.
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Karkhi denied description suffices; Abu Yusuf allowed it for all items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وصح عقد الأعمى) أي بيعه وشراؤه وسائر عقوده لأنه مكلف محتاج اليها فصار كالبصير، ولتعامل الناس له من غير نكير فصار بمنزله الإجماع وبه قال الأئمة الثلاثة وقد كتبت في الفوائد أن الأعمى كالبصير إلا في مسائل : لا جهاد عليه ولا جمعه ولا جماعه حج وإن وجد قائداً في الكل، ولا يصلح كونه شاهداً ولو فيما تقبل فيه الشهادة بالتسامح على المذاهب ولا دية في عينيه وإنما الواجب حكومة عدل، وكره أذانه وحده وإمامته إلا أن يكون أعلم القوم، ولا يجوز إعتاقه عن الكفارات ولا كونه إماماً أعظم ولا قاضياً، ويكره ذبحه ولم أر حكم صيده ورميه وإجتهاده في القبلة قوله : (وسقط خياره إذا اشترى بجس المبيع وشمه وذوقه وفي العقار بوصفه لأن هذه الأشياء تفيد العلم لمن استعملها على ما بينا في البصير. والمراد بسقوطه سقوطه إذا وجدت هذه الأشياء قبل الشراء ثم اشترى، وأما إذا اشترى قبل هذه فهذه مثبته للخيار له لا لأنها مسقطة. ويمتد أن يوجد منه ما يدل على الرضا من قول وفعل في الصحيح وعبارة الولوالجية أن هذه الأشياء منه بمنزلة النظر من البصير. وقوله بجس المبيع معناه أن كان مما يجس وشمه إن كان مما يشمل والذوق فيما يذاق باللسان، وأما إذا اشترى عقاراً فرؤيته بوصفه له في جامع الفتاوى هو يوقف في مكان لو كان بصير الرآه ثم يذكر صفته، ولا يخفى أن إيقافه في ذلك المكان ليس شرطاً في صحة الوصف وسقوط الخيار به ولذا لم يذكره في المبسوط، واكتفى بذكر الوصف لأنه أقيم مقام الرؤية في السلم. ممن أنكره الكرخي وقال : وقوفه في ذلك الموضوع وغيره سواء في أنه لا يستفيد بذلك علماً ؛ كذا في فتح القدير وظاهر ما في الكتاب أن الوصف إنما يكتفي به العقار وأن غيره لا يوصف له. وعن أبي يوسف اعتبار الوصف في غير العقار أيضاً وظاهره أيضاً أنه لا شرط مع الوصف في العقار. وقال مشايخ بلخ: يمس الحيطان والأشجار. وظاهره أيضاً أن الجس فيما عدا ما يشم ويذاق والعقار. واستثنى منه في فتح
His saying: (And the contract of the blind person is valid) i.e., his sale, purchase, and all other contracts, because he is a responsible person in need of them, so he is like the sighted person; and due to people dealing with him without disapproval, so it is like consensus, and the three Imams said so. And it is written in al-Fawa'id that the blind person is like the sighted person except in some issues: no jihad upon him, nor Friday prayer, nor congregation for Hajj even if he finds a guide in all; he is not suitable as a witness even in cases where testimony by tolerance is accepted according to the schools; no diyah for his eyes, rather the obligation is a fair compensation; his adhan alone is disliked, and his imamate unless he is the most knowledgeable of the people; it is not permitted to free slaves as expiation, nor to be a supreme imam, nor a judge; his slaughter is disliked; and I have not seen the ruling on his hunting, shooting, and ijtihad regarding the qiblah. His saying: (And his option falls if he buys by touching the sold item, smelling it, and tasting it, and in real estate by its description) because these things provide knowledge for the one who uses them, as we explained for the sighted person. And what is meant by 'falls' is that it falls if these things occur before the purchase then he buys; but if he buys before these, then these establish the option for him, not because they are causes for falling. And it is extended that there occur from him something indicating satisfaction, by word or deed, in the sound view. And the expression of al-Walwaljiah is that these things from him are like sight from the sighted person. And his saying 'by touching the sold item' means if it is something that can be touched; 'smelling it' if it is something that can be smelled; 'tasting' is for what is tasted by the tongue. And if he buys real estate, his seeing it is by its description. In Jami' al-Fatawa, he is made to stand in a place where, were he sighted, he would see it, then he describes it. It is not hidden that making him stand in that place is not a condition for the validity of the description and the falling of the option by it, and for that reason it was not mentioned in al-Mabsut, and it sufficed with mentioning the description because it was established in the place of sight in salam. From al-Karkhi: he denied it and said: his standing in that place or elsewhere is the same in that he does not gain knowledge from it. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And the apparent meaning of what is in the book is that description is relied upon only for real estate, and that other things are not described for him. And from Abu Yusuf, consideration of description in other than real estate also. And his apparent also that there is no condition along with description for real estate. And the scholars of Balkh said: He touches the walls and trees. And his apparent also that touching is for other than what is smelled, tasted, and real estate. And he excluded from it in Fath
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage rules on the validity of contracts performed by a blind person and the means by which a blind buyer may exercise khiyar al-ru'yah. The author first establishes that a blind person's contracts (sale, purchase, etc.) are valid because he is a morally responsible person with needs, same as a sighted person. This is supported by the fact that people have dealt with blind individuals without objection, making it akin to consensus. The text then lists exceptions where the blind person is not treated like the sighted: no obligation of jihad, Friday prayer, or Hajj in congregation; not qualified as a witness; no full diyah for his eyes (only fair compensation); disliked to give adhan alone or lead prayer unless most knowledgeable; not allowed to free slaves for expiation; cannot be caliph or judge; slaughter by blind is disliked. The core ruling relates to khiyar al-ru'yah for the blind: his option falls when he touches, smells, or tastes the sold item (if such senses apply), and for real estate, through a detailed description. These actions substitute for sight. The option falls only if these actions occur before purchase; if done after purchase, they do not cause fall but rather indicate approval. There is a disagreement with al-Karkhi who denied that description suffices. Abu Yusuf extended description to non-real estate. The preferred view is that description is sufficient for real estate, and for other items, the blind person may touch, smell, or taste as applicable.

Key Terms
الأعمى (al-a'ma): A person who is blind from birth or later; in this context, a buyer or seller in a contract.
الجس (al-jass): Touching or feeling the sold item to ascertain its quality; a substitute for sight for the blind.
الوصف (al-wasf): A verbal description of a property, used for real estate when determining khiyar al-ru'yah for a blind person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A blind person buys a house without seeing it. How does he exercise his khiyar al-ru'yah?
A) He must physically visit and touch the walls.
B) He must have someone describe the house to him.
C) He cannot have khiyar al-ru'yah because he is blind.
D) He automatically loses the option once the sale is concluded.

Key Takeaway
A blind person's contract is valid, and his khiyar al-ru'yah is exercised through touch, smell, taste, or description, with the option falling only if these occur before purchase.
